Approval

neering Solid Mechanics, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial fulfilment for
the degree Master of Science in Engineering, MSc Eng.
It is assumed that the reader has a basic knowledge in the areas of dynamics and vibrations.
Rasmus Birch Drewsen ­ s163158

ii
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This master’s thesis delves into the subject of rotor­stator contact dynamics. Rotor­stator appli­
cations have widespread use in the industry and the contact dynamics of backup bearings are of
great interest, to prevent catastrophic failure. The research tested and validated friction and normal
force models for nonlinear rotor­stator contact dynamics, utilizing a previously developed model
and test­rig. Additionally, the study involves improving data handling and simulation control,
assessing the state and repeatability of the test­rig, estimating contact parameters, exploring mul­
tiple materials, and identify sources of issues in the experimental results. Eleven contact models
based on the penalty method, including two new ones, were implemented. Seven friction mod­
els, six static and one dynamic, were implemented. The friction force models were compared to
each other on a modified setup on the test rig, without the Permanent Magnetic Bearing PMB and
with an applied load. The research has been heavily experimental and multiple impact and friction
tests for different bushing types were performed. impacts with angular velocity of Ω= 0 Hz, and
friction tests were performed on brass, bronze, and PTFE bushing types. Testing for impacts with
angular velocity of Ω= 17 Hz was performed on brass and bronze. The repeatability was assessed
with the mean and Standard Deviation (SD). An experimental estimate of the Coefficient of Resti­
tution (CoR) was made by differentiation of the rotor’s position data. The sources of cross­couple
stiffnesses were investigated, by comparing results with and without the couplings for a full rota­
tion, analyzed in 45­degree increments. The introduction of a Graphical User Interface (GUI) as
well as an automatic data extraction of the LabView data from the test­rig, significantly sped up
the simulation process. Comparing the normal force models to a non­smooth model revealed that
the newer models were accurate in the entire range if CoR, With the ’Yu et. al’ being most accurate
anywhere but for cr = 0.25 and the ’Safaeifar & Farshidianfar’ and ’Gonthier’ were also close.
However, the comparison of the simulation with the experimental data led to the conclusion that the
’Herbert & McWhannell’ model would be the preferred model at a cr = 0.65 due to comparable
results at less computational cost. The experimental results for the friction tests did not align with
the expected modeled behavior, thus a new test­setup is needed for comparing the different models.
However, for high relative velocity impacts the simplest model like the ’Ambrosio’ would be best.
The experimental estimation of the CoR revealed minute differences between the bushings, and it
was concluded that a steel sleeve could make for more of a difference. The CoR was found to be
approximately cr ≈0.65. The couplings provide a worsening of the cross­coupling effect, while
not being the only source. The experimental and simulated orbit were out of sync and a new orbit
fit showed a restored synchronicity, while the fitting parameters were insufficient to fit the orbit
for multiple angular velocities. The discrepancies between the experimental and simulation results
were highly influenced by the limitations of the test­rig, mainly with, the Data Acquisition System
(DAS), the lag of contact detection, and the unreplicable higher frequency vibrations. A contact
signal was implemented. However, the current iteration presented issues with electrical isolation
and fast impact detection. Furthermore, the orbit data demonstrated a degradation in repeatability
compared to earlier results, likely due to wear on the spherical bearing and electrical interference,
which also affected the magnet activation angle. The conclusions presented in this thesis should
present a solid foundation for future improvements to the test­rig.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics

Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
1.1
Background & Motivation
Rotor­stator systems are used in a variety of applications within the field of mechanical engineer­
ing. In every rotating machine, there will be a spinning rotor and a stationary stator. In such a
system catastrophic failure could occur if the rotor unintentionally touches the stator. Contact can
happen for numerous reasons: if the system is disturbed by an external force, bearing failure due to
power loss of an active magnetic bearing, or failure of machine elements due to excessive loading
or fatigue. These events are high consequence as it is extremely dangerous to be around rotating
machinery during catastrophic failure. Furthermore, it is costly to repair. It is thus beneficial to
have redundancies in a system to make sure if one part fail, another can take over. Backup bear­
ings do exactly that, they are used to catch the shaft in the event of an external force that exceeds
the holding force of a magnetic bearing or if an active magnetic bearing loses power. The contact
dynamics that occur during an impact are therefore of great interest, as they can help to understand
what happens if a backup bearing comes into contact with the rotor. This knowledge can be used to
avoid events like the backwards annular whirl, which sends the rotor into an uncontrollable whirl
around the bearing.
In rotor dynamics, the rotor and the stator are often modeled using the Finite Element Method
(FEM). The contact force can be divided into two components, the force in the direction normal
to the impact and the force in the tangential direction to the impact, which are the normal force
and friction force, respectively. Considerable research has been done on the calculation of the
normal force during contact. The main methods are the nonsmooth method and the penalty method.
The nonsmooth method has been shown to be more accurate in the literature. However, it is also
significantly more computationally intensive. The penalty method uses assumptions to formulate
constitutive equations for estimating the contact force. Numerous models have been developed
over the years, in an attempt to provide a more accurate estimate for a larger range of Coefficient
of Restitution (CoR), where many are based on the Hertzian theory. The tangential force can be
related to the friction occurring in the contact between the rotor and the stator. Understanding how
the normal force, relative velocity and coefficient of friction influences the friction force is thus
of high importance. Multiple friction models have been developed over the years, both static and
dynamic models, with newer models implementing increasingly complex frictional phenomena
such as pre­sliding and dwell time. It is of interest to compare these models in the context of
rotor­stator impact dynamics to simulate the impact most accurately.
1.2
Project Scope
The main objective of this thesis is to organize the collective work made in [1],[2] and [3] into
one program including everything from data processing to simulation, improving upon each of the
elements where possible and identifying areas that needs to be reworked. During this process the
normal force models introduced in [2] as well as the friction force models introduced in [3] are to
be validated. It is the objective to validate the models using the test­rig, as time constraints would
make it difficult to design and build new test setups. The testing will also be used to estimate
the necessary parameters for simulating the contact response, like the CoR and the coefficient of
friction. Furthermore, a new bushing material will be implemented to have data on more different
impacts. Finally, a comparison of the contact dynamics will be performed using the knowledge
gained from the validation and testing. Experimental testing is at the heart of the project and the
Data Acquisition System (DAS) is getting thoroughly investigated. Statistical tools will be used to
validate the repeatability of the test­rig results. The Fast Fourier Transform (FFT) will be used to
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
1
==PAGE:12==
get familiar with the frequencies present in the experimental results. The Graphical User Interface
(GUI) will be developed in MATLABTM’s AppDesigner, which plays well with the existing code
base in MATLABTM.
Improvements to the test­rig itself are out of scope for this project, as it will be better suited for a
project where the focus can be on the mechanical design of the test­rig. However, the results from
this thesis should lead to some insights into where the test­rig needs improvements and what could
possibly be done. As the focus of the project is mainly on the experimental side, modifications to
the already established mathematical model will not be performed.
1.3
The Experimental Setup
When analyzing any dynamic problem, it is important to validate the mathematical model with
experimental results. The simulated model will be built on a set of assumptions that simplify the
calculations. To ensure the validity of these assumptions it is critical to compare the results to
experimental data throughout the process. For this purpose, a rotor­stator test­rig has been built.
The test­rig was originally constructed in 2011 as part of a master thesis [4]. It has since gone
through multiple design iterations, it was fundamentally redesigned as part of a special course in
2018 [5]. In 2019, as part of a master thesis [1], the two polycarbonate housings where modified to
decrease friction due to poor tolerances, the mounting of the Permanent Magnetic Bearing (PMB)
was also revised, and the excitation device using a magnetic spool was finalized. In 2020 as part
of a bachelor thesis [2], two additional accelerometers were added to account for the flexibility of
the inner and middle housing.
Figure 1.1: CAD model of the rotor­stator contact test­rig. Key components: (A) Impact house.
(B) Unbalance disc. (C) PMB. (D) External excitation device. (E) Spherical bearing. (F) Coupling.
(G) Motor.
The test­rig consists of a rotor and a stator. The rotor consists of an aluminum shaft with various
machine elements mounted to it. The stator is mounted in the inner house where it aligns with the
end of the shaft. Contact will occur between the stator and the sleeve. Figure 1.1 illustrates the
various sub­assemblies of the test­rig, each essential for producing the desired results:
2
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:13==
• Impact House (A): Measures horizontal and vertical acceleration and force for calculating
the normal and tangential forces of a rotor­stator impact.
• Unbalance Disc (B): Introduces a variable unbalance and serves as the measurement point
for the position of the rotor.
• PMB (C): Levitates the rotor to minimize the friction typically associated with mechanical
bearings.
• External Excitation Device (D): A solenoid­operated magnetic spool that can apply a pulling
force on the shaft via a ferromagnetic sleeve, generating an impact.
• Spherical Bearing (E): Constrains longitudinal movement while allowing minor rotations.
• Coupling (F): Transfers torque from the motor while reducing the bending moment and
accommodating small misalignments.
• Motor (G): Drives the rotor and continuously measures the rotor angle with a rotary encoder
mounted on the back of the motor shaft.
The impact house consists of an outer casing made from steel, grounding the whole housing, a poly­
carbonate middle house that is constraint to move horizontally, and a polycarbonate inner house
that is constraint to move vertically, relative to the middle house. By measuring how much these
houses accelerate and using their known mass the inertia can be calculated. Using this data in com­
bination with measurements from four force transducers in a north, south, east, west configuration,
the force of an impact can be calculated. The force transducers used are the unidirectional C9B
from Hottinger Baldwin Messtechnik (HBM), now re­branded to Hottinger, Brüel & Kjær (HBK)
after a merger with Brüel & Kjær.
Figure 1.2: Front view of the impact house
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
3
==PAGE:14==
Figure 1.2 presents a front view of the impact house. The west and east force transducers are
mounted on the steel frame, measuring the horizontal force when the middle house moves on the
horizontal linear steel rods. Conversely, the north and south force transducers are mounted in
the middle house, measuring the forces in the vertical direction when the inner house moves on
the vertical linear steel rods, which are fixed to the middle house. The force transducers are pre­
tensioned using four brass bolts. This pre­tensioning is essential because the force transducers
are unidirectional; by applying pre­tension, the transducers can effectively measure force in both
directions. The stator is designed to be modular, allowing different bushings and bearings into
the inner house to alter the characteristics of the impact. Furthermore, the rotor is fitted with an
interchangeable contact sleeve, making it possible to modify both materials in the contact pair. Two
accelerometers are mounted to each house. One for the unconstrained movement, and one in the
constraint direction, to account for flex in the polycarbonate or guide rails. The KS77C10 model
from Metra Mess­ und Frequenztechnik (MMF), is mounted at the bottom of the inner house to
measure acceleration in its unconstrained direction, along the guide rods displayed in green. The
KS77C100 model from MMF, is mounted to the right side of the middle house, (as oriented in
Figure 1.2), to measure acceleration in its unconstrained direction along the guide rods displayed
in blue. The KD38 from MMF is used for both extra accelerometers. One is mounted to the stator
bushing on the inner house, the other is mounted to the bottom of the middle house.
There will be some intrinsic residual unbalance in the rotor components due to inaccuracies in
manufacturing. However, the unbalanced disc is a machine element added to the rotor to control
the unbalance of the rotor system. It is fitted with threaded holes allowing for bolts to be inserted,
changing the unbalance response. Furthermore, it is on the disc that the orbit is measured. The
displacement of the rotor in the horizontal and vertical directions is measured using two eddy
current proximity sensors. These are the KJ8­M18MN50­ANU from Pulsotronic.
The initiation of an impact is managed without physical contact with the rotor using a solenoid­
activated magnetic spool of the Type GMH from MAGNET­SCHULTZ. It exerts a pulling force on
the rotor via a ferromagnetic sleeve on the shaft. The pulling force is measured using a bidirectional
force transducer, the U9B from HBM. By synchronizing the angle, this data can be implemented
in the simulation to exert the same force on the digital twin.
The rotor is suspended by two types of bearings, the spherical bearing and the PMB. The reason
for this is that the main purpose of the test­rig is to measure the contact dynamics, and the overall
efficiency is thus not of importance. Hence, one PMB is sufficient to emulate the backup bearing
contact. The spherical bearing serves to constrain the translational Degrees Of Freedom (DOF),
while maintaining rotational freedom.
The diaphragm couplings are used to transfer torque while dampening the torsional vibration and
allowing for slight misalignment between the rotor and the motor shaft. The Ø 14mm steel shaft
is added to reduce the amount of bending moments transferred by the motor. A rotary encoder of
the type 2400 from Kübler, is mounted at the back of the motor to track the angle of the rotor shaft.
The angle data is used to synchronize each hit to the same angle as well as give a reference for the
simulation angle for calculating the unbalance response.

the length of the shaft as well as the lengths from the coupling end to the various machine elements.

1.4
Data Acquisition System
The goal of extracting information about an impact of the backup bearing/bushing on the test­rig
requires some form of DAS. As mentioned in section 1.3, there are multiple sensors on the test­rig.
These sensors needs to be connected to a computer through a Data Acquisition Unit (DAU). The
DAU utilized in the test­rig is the multifunction I/O device; USB­6363, from National Instruments
(NI). It is able to handle up to 2MSPS and has 48 I/O’s. Figure 1.4 depicts a block diagram illus­
trating how the DAS is configured, a more detailed overview of where the wires are connected to
the DAU can be found in Appendix B.
Figure 1.4: Block Diagram of the DAS
A program has been developed in [1] using NI’s LabVIEW [6] to extract and display the data from
the DAU as well as saving it to a TDMS file format for later processing in Matlab [7]. The interface
is depicted in Figure 1.5. The interface allows for easy changes to the sample rate, filename, folder
6
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:17==
and impact duration, i.e. the duration of the signal to the magnetic spool. It also allows for toggling
the function of hit synchronisation, which for a spinning rotor keeps the the unbalance position
consistent between impacts. Finally, it displays the sensor data in real time for each of the sensors,
the position sensors have been combined in an XY graph to show the orbit of the center of the
rotor. The interface has been rearranged to fit the addition of a new signal, handling the contact
detection, this implementation is explained in section 4.1.4.
Figure 1.5: UI of the LabView program
The signals from the force transducer and accelerometers have been processed through a signal
conditioner before going to the DAU. These signal conditioners perform filtering and amplifica­
tion. An analogue low­pass filter is utilized to eliminate high frequency noise before sampling, to
avoid aliasing effects. The AE101 by HBM used for the force transducers applies a 6 kHz cut­
off frequency, the M33 used for the two main accelerometers, employs a 5kHz cutoff frequency.
Meanwhile, the charge amplifiers used for the two additional accelerometers, as introduced in [2],
uses a 10kHz cutoff frequency. According to the Nyquist­Shannon theorem, a continuous sig­
nal with no frequencies above the Nyquist frequency 1/fs, where fs is the sampling frequency,
can be converted to a discrete signal without loss of information. Consequently, this theorem de­
mands that the sample rate should be at least twice the highest frequency present in the signal.
Although the 10kHz cutoff ensures that higher frequencies are removed, due to the filter’s roll­off,
it is advisable to over­sample. Therefore, a sample rate of 24kHz or higher should be reasonable.
However, as the DAU can handle even higher sample rates, and to increase the resolution of the
signal, especially for the fast impulse of the impact event, a sample rate of 48kHz has been set
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
7
==PAGE:18==
during testing.
Amplification of the signal plays a crucial role in optimizing the output voltage range based on the
input voltage applied by the load. The sensors have an inherent nominal sensitivity, which cannot
be altered, making it important to correctly dimension them for a given application. However, due
to the test­rig’s utilization in multiple projects, including a Ph.D. study [8] on backward annular
whirl with high unbalance, and the master thesis [1] with significantly lower forces using a mag­
netic spool for excitation, the input voltage range varies substantially. Given the high cost of these
sensors, replacing them is not feasible. Therefore, it is necessary to adjust the sensitivity through
amplification. This approach provides better resolution by utilizing the whole voltage range of the
DAU. However, it also amplifies the noise, and care must be taken not to exceed the DAU’s input
range, as this could lead to saturation and clipping of the signal. The nominal sensitivity as well
as the sensitivity setting chosen in the signal conditioners by [1] are presented in Table 1.4, along
with an overview of the different sensors and signal conditioners used. The data sheets on these
sensors can be found in appendix A.

2
Modeling
This chapter will focus on describing the digital twin that has been developed to simulate the
response of the test­rig during a rotor­stator impact. The FEM has been used to model the shaft
of the rotor. In the end a global matrix system of Ordinary Differential Equations (ODE) will be
built with all the DOF of the rotor and stator.
The rotor and stator are coupled via the contact models, based on the penalty method. The gap
between the rotor and stator is dynamically calculated and used to determine when contact occurs.
Chapter 3 goes into more detail on the contact dynamics.
˙x = f(x),
x ∈Rn
(2.1)
2.1
Model of Impact Housing
The impact house model, in [1], was originally modelled with four DOF. 2 DOF in the vertical
direction and 2 DOF in the horizontal direction. However, in [2] the model was expanded to 5
DOF. The vertical direction still has 2 DOF while the horizontal direction has 3 DOF. By dividing
the middle house into two equivalently sized bodies connected with springs and dampers.
(a) Horizontal direction
(b) Vertical direction
Figure 2.1: Schematic representation of the impact housing
Figure 2.1a depicts the schematic representation of the system in the horizontal direction. The
figure illustrates the 3 bodies, one for the inner house and two for the middle house. Equivalent
springs and dampers are introduced to represent the system. The two middle house bodies each get
half of the equivalent stiffness kvb and damping dvb of the guiding rods. The left body is in contact
with the western force transducer and the right body is in contact with the eastern force transducer,
each represented with an equivalent stiffness kft2 and damping dft2. Finally the torsion stiffness
ktm and damping dtm is shared on the two bodies. These are used to make the model capable of
modelling the twisting mode shape. The mass of the inner house is denoted mih and the mass of
the middle house is denoted mmh which is split equally between the two halves.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
9
==PAGE:20==
Figure 2.1b presents a schematic representation of the system in the vertical direction. This di­
rection is considerably simpler since the equivalent stiffnesses, and damping coefficients can be
superposed. The inner housing makes contact with both the north and south force transducers,
resulting in a combined effect of twice the equivalent stiffness kft1 and damping dft1. The guide
rods that pass through the middle housing introduce additional equivalent stiffness khb and damp­
ing dhb. The masses of the middle and inner housings are denoted as mmh and mih, respectively.

2.2
Model of Rotor
The rotor consists of various machine elements mounted to a shaft. The shaft is modelled as a
flexible body with distributed mass. FEM is used to build the model. The shaft will be supported
at two nodes, one representing the spherical bearing and the other representing the PMB. The
support will be implemented by introducing stiffness and dampening in these nodes. Proportional
damping, also called Rayleigh damping, is also implemented to model internal structural damping.
To construct the FEM model, the rotor has been discretized. In [1], a convergence study was
conducted to determine the optimal discretization of the rotor. Although a relatively coarse dis­
cretization could meet the criterion of a maximum error of 2% in the natural frequencies as defined
in that report, a discretization using 16 elements was chosen. It provides sufficient discretization
to enable the mounting of all machine elements while maintaining nearly uniform element lengths.
This is done to improve the stability of the numerical model. The discretization of the rotor as well
as the nodes at which the machine elements are mounted can be seen in figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Discretization of the rotor
2.2.1
FEM of Shaft
A series of assumptions have been made to model the complex physical system with a relatively
computationally inexpensive numerical model. The assumptions for the shaft elements are given
below:
12
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:23==
Assumptions of Shaft Elements
• Flexible
• The spherical bearing completely constrains movement along the longitudinal axis.
• The Shaft is isotropic and symmetric around the longitudinal axis of rotation, geometric
features like keyways are ignored.
• Euler bernoullis beam theory is used thus small deflections are assumed.
• The system is modelled conservative, hence, no dissipation of energy in the rotor. ­ dissipa­
tion is handled by Rayleigh damping
• Lateral vibrations are dominant, thus torsional vibrations are neglected.
Figure 2.5: Coordinate system used for the rotor
Figure 2.5 illustrates the orientation of the coordinate system for the mathematical FE­model,
where V and W denote the DOFs in the horizontal and vertical directions, respectively. The an­
gular velocity, Ω, is controlled by the motor and is rotating at a constant speed. β and Γ represent
the DOFs for rotation around the y­axis and z­axis, respectively. These four DOFs can be used at
each end of a Finite Element to create an 8­DOF element.

2.2.2
Model of Machine Elements
The machine elements need to be modelled mathematically so they can be mounted at the corre­
sponding nodes in the FEM model. Most of the machine elements can be modelled as rigid discs.
the following assumptions have been made when considering these machine elements:
Assumptions of Discs
• Rigid disc, e.i. they store no potential energy.
• Isotropic properties and rotational symmetry.
• Conservative, no dissipation of energy.
• Infinitely thin, hence they provide no stiffness to the shaft.
The EOM for the rigid disc is calculated in a similar fashion to the shaft element analysis, using
Lagrangian energy formulation. With no potential energy present, the formula becomes:
Md¨q(t)d −ΩGd ˙q(t)d = Qd
(2.17)
where, Md is the mass matrix for the discs and Gd is the gyroscopic matrix for the discs.
Finally, the bearings are to be modelled. They are modelled by a series of springs and dampers.
The following assumptions have been made when considering the bearings:

Assumptions of bearings
• Bearings are modelled without mass
• Constant bearing elasticity i.e. equivalence to a linear spring.
• Viscous damping

2.2.3
Implementation of Rayleigh Damping to Rotor
The rotor shaft and discs are modeled conservatively, meaning they have no ability to dissipate
energy. To impart the model with the ability to dissipate energy, in addition to the damping provided
by the bearings, proportional damping, also known as Rayleigh damping, is applied:
D = αM + βK
(2.20)
As can be seen in equation (2.20), the damping matrix D is proportional with the mass matrix M
through the proportionality constant α and with the stiffness matrix K through the proportionality
constant β.
The proportionality constants where estimated in an Experimental Modal Analysis (EMA) per­
formed in [1] and can be seen in table 2.1. However, due to the implementation of damping
through the PMB, it was decided not to introduce damping of the rigid body motions through
Rayleigh damping and the Rayleigh damping was made proportional to the stiffness matrix only,
i.e., α = 0.
α/β
α
β
10.57/3.53 · 10−7
12.32
2.48 · 10−6
Table 2.1: Damping proportionality constants found in an EMA in [1]
2.2.4
Implementation of Unbalance to Rotor
The rotor shaft and machine elements have, up to this point, been assumed to be perfectly ax­
isymmetric and isotropic, implying balance. However, this does not reflect the real world, where
manufacturing processes invariably come with inherent inaccuracies. Therefore, to simulate the
overall imbalance of the entire rotor, an imbalance is introduced at the node of the unbalanced disc.
This is represented through a lumped mass m0 positioned a radial distance ε from the center axis
at a specific phase angle from the zero angle reference, of the shaft, illustrated in Figure 2.6.

2.3
Building the Global Model
Figure 2.7 illustrates how the global matrix of size N × N is built, with N representing the total
number of DOF of the shaft. Each element shares a node with the neighboring elements, except the
first and last node. This is illustrated by the crisscross pattern in the figure. The machine elements
are mounted in their corresponding nodes. The mass matrix for the coupling is placed at the 16th
node, giving nonzero elements on the diagonal starting from DOF 65.
Figure 2.7: Illustration of the global mass matrix
The global system for the rotor can be mathematically expressed by:
Mr¨qr + (−ΩGr + Dr)˙qr + Krqr = Qr
(2.27)
where qr stores all the 68 DOF of the rotor and Mr, Kr, Gr, Dr and Qr are the mass matrix,
stiffness matrix, gyroscopic matrix, damping matrix and external force vector of the global rotor
system, respectively.

2.4
1 DOF Model
In the following section an analytical model will be developed to predict the dynamics of the rotor
as it moves freely on the stator wall when the PMB is deactivated. The purpose of this model
is to compare the different friction models with the test rig results. The test rig will have to be
modified to make these test, these modifications are explained in section 4.1.3. To construct this
model a moving reference frame has been applied to the rotor. This reference frame is attached in
the Center of Mass (CM) of the rotor and is rotated with the angle θ to always align with the path
of the rotor. The Inertial reference frame is denoted with an I and the moving reference frame is
denoted with a B. A system diagram of the model can be seen in Figure 2.8.
Figure 2.8: System diagram for friction model ­ clearance has been exaggerated for better clarity
Assumptions
The following assumptions where made when developing the model:
• The use of Newtons law assumes the rotor as a particle
• Distance between rotor and stator center is fixed
• The stator center is stationary
• The rotation axis for the moving reference frame zb are parallel to the rotation axis of the
inertial reference frame z
• Any unbalance has been ignored, the geometric center and the center of mass coincide
• The motor can deliver enough torque to produce the calculated friction force
• Rolling resistance is ignored
Although it is a single DOF model defined in two­dimensions, the model is derived using a vector­
based approach in three­dimensional space. This methodology is detailed in [10]. It allows for
future expansion of the model, to incorporate additional degrees of freedom or alternative assump­
tions.

Contact Dynamics
This chapter is concerned with briefly explaining the forces involved in the contact dynamics in a
rotor­stator impact. The models used to simulate a rotor­stator impact, along with their constitutive
laws will be presented.

3.1
Normal Force
The normal force that occurs as two objects collide in an impact has been covered widely in the
literature. Many authors have put their spin on a penalty method that can reliably and computa­
tionally effectively estimate the hysteresis damping factor.
3.1.1
Normal Force Models
The simplest elastic contact force model is Hooke’s law.
FN = kδ
(3.10)
The expression above is attributed to (Shigley and Mischke) [11] in (Flores and Hamid) [12]. It
relates the normal force to the relative penetration of the colliding objects, through a spring stiffness
k. It is a purely linear relationship, and the spring stiffness can be hard to evaluate.
24
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:35==
Heinrich Hertz contact model from 1881 [13] introduced a nonlinearity that better captured the
dynamics of an impact:
FN = Kδn
(3.11)
where the generalized stiffness parameter is given:
K =
4
3 (σi + σj)
s
RiRj
Ri + Rj
(3.12)
the material parameters σi and σj are given by
σl = 1 −v2
l
El
,
(l = i, j)
(3.13)
δ is the relative penetration or indentation. The exponent of the nonlinearity n has been found to
be equal to 3/2 for the case where there is a parabolic distribution of contact stresses [12].
(Kelvin and Voigt) [14] proposed one of the first dissipative contact force models. This model was
based on Hookes law adding a linear damper element in parallel with the spring. The constitutive
law has the following expression:
FN = Kδ + D ˙δ
(3.14)
K is the stiffness parameter, δ denotes the relative penetration or deformation, D is the damping
coefficient of the damper, and ˙δ represents the relative normal contact velocity.
The benefit of the Kelvin and Voigt approach is that it takes dissipative effects into account. How­
ever, it is a linear approach and due to the way damping is implemented it behaves nonphysical.
To improve upon the Hertzian contact theory, equation 3.11 was expanded with a term to account
for the dissipative effects. This was done by (Hunt and Crossley) [15] in 1975, the constitutive law
became:
FN = Kδn + D ˙δ,
D = χδn,
χ = hr
K
˙δ−,
hr = 3(1 −cr)
2
(3.15)
where χ is the hysteresis factor. It is calculated using the hysteresis damping ratio, the impacting
velocity ˙δ−and the stiffness K. The hysteresis damping ratio is calculated using the CoR, denoted
with cr.
”The classical definition of ”coefficient of restitution” e is, in simple one­dimensional impact be­
tween two rigid bodies in pure translation, the ratio of their relative speed after impact to that
before.” [15].
cr =
˙δ+
˙δ−
(3.16)
It is dependent on the geometry at impact, the materials, and the relative velocity of the impact.
15 contact models based on these different approaches were considered in the a previous bachelor
project [2]. However, the ones chosen for further investigation and the ones implemented into the

difference lies in how they each calculate the hysteresis damping ratio. Since the work by (Frederik
& Andreas) [2], new models have been formed to better describe the contact force. A multitude of

3.2
Friction Force
Friction is an unavoidable physical phenomenon affecting any dynamic system. Sometimes friction
is beneficial, for example as a holding force. However, friction is often undesired, and there is a
need to make efforts to reduce it. This is often done by utilizing fluid lubrication film between
machine elements. However, in the impact between the rotor and stator, implementing such a
lubrication film is infeasible and it is thus important to understand the dry friction that occurs in
such an impact.
In Tribology, friction is categorized into two main types: dry friction, which is the opposing force
26
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:37==
between two solid bodies in relative motion, and wet friction, which is the opposing force between
a solid body and a fluid in relative motion. Given the unlubricated nature of the impact, the primary
focus is on dry friction. There are four main types of friction: static friction, kinetic friction (also
known as dynamic friction or sliding friction), rolling friction, and fluid friction.
3.2.1
Micro­Mechanisms of Friction
According to adhesion theory by Bowden and Tabor [19], the main mechanism causing friction
between two unlubricated surfaces is the adhesion which occurs at the region of real contact. Ad­
hesion refers to the welding of two connecting asperities trough interfacial bonding, where the
surface of the asperities are held together by intermolecular forces. To understand the concept of
asperities and the real contact region one must zoom into a microscopic level.
Based on a recent review of dry friction models by Euser et al. [20], it becomes apparent that
the precise mechanisms of friction have yet to be completely agreed upon within the literature.
Nonetheless, the theory concerning the interaction of asperities is agreed upon as a foundational
concept.
Figure 3.2: Micro­mechanisms of friction: Contact interface of the asperities of a contact pair in
relative motion
The inherent roughness of any surface, regardless of how smooth it appears, will lead to micro­
scopic asperities on the surface. These asperities are what come into contact during dry friction.
Figure 3.2 provides a visual representation of the cross section of two solid objects in contact. The
divided zones within the figure illustrate the various mechanisms during a dry sliding event, ac­
cording to adhesion theory. Velocity increases from left to right. Initially, as the surfaces make
contact, the asperities will deform elasto­plastically, resulting in a resistance to movement. Then,
as relative movement increases micro welding between the contacting asperities occur, fusing them
together at the tip. As relative motion increases, they undergo plastic deformation and eventually
shear off, where the friction transitions to sliding friction.
Ploughing is another mechanism that contributes to friction. It occurs when harder asperities
’plough through’ softer asperities instead of adhering to them. Some experimental cases show that
adhesion is not always the primary factor in dry friction and that the ploughing effect can sometimes
be a more significant contributor, as demonstrated in the work done by Walton [21]. However, a
work­hardening effect on the material will lead to a decrease in the influence of ploughing over
time.
According to Bowden [19], adhesion occurs at the region of real contact and sliding can only occur
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
27
==PAGE:38==
once the welded asperities are deformed or sheared. The plowing term can be added, assuming
negligible interaction between the two effects and thus the friction force can be expressed as:
F = As + P
(3.17)
where A is the real contact area and s is the shear strength of the welded asperities and P is the
ploughing term. The real area of contact plays a vital role in the magnitude of the friction force. It
was concluded that for any situation where elastic deformation occurs the real contact area has the
following relationship with the applied load: A ∝W m, where W is the load and m lies between
2
3 and 1.
The main contributors to the friction force is the normal force, the sliding velocity, and the surface
roughness.
Olsson [22] and Al­Bender [23] describes friction as two regimes with a transition between them.
They describe the first regime as being the pre­sliding regime, where the asperities deform elasto­
plastically. This regime is dominated by relative displacement and is the static friction, often re­
ferred to in its abbreviated form as stiction. Stiction characterizes the friction that occurs before
sliding is initiated. The breakaway force is the amount of force required to overcome the static
friction coefficient and set the interacting surfaces in motion. Conversely dynamic friction is the
force opposing the relative motion of the sliding pair. It is the force required to overcome the
kinetic friction coefficient and maintain a slide. The kinetic friction coefficient is velocity depen­
dent, and it is usually lower than the static friction coefficient. In other words, it takes more force
to overcome stiction and initiate a slide than it does to maintain one. The second regime is the
gross sliding. However, there is a transition in between the two regimes referred to as the stick­slip
regime or often just the transition regime. The main complication in modeling friction occurs in the
pre­sliding regime as well as in the transition, which is at low relative velocity. Many complicated
phenomenons occur here, such as the stribeck effect, hysteresis, and dwell time. Different models
have been developed over the years to include an increasing amount of these phenomena.
3.2.2
Friction Force Models
A total of eleven friction force models have been investigated in the bachelor project [3]. Six of the
static models have been implemented successfully and one dynamic model, the LuGre model, has
been implemented without success. The models will be briefly explained in the following section.
Static Models
Classic Coulomb
The classic Coulomb friction model is the simplest of the friction models and it dates back to 1785
[24]. It models both static friction and dynamic friction through a piecewise function with the
following branches:
F
(
≤µsFn
v = 0
= µdFn sgn(v)
v ̸= 0
(3.18)
where Fn denotes the normal force, µs is the static coefficient of friction, µd is the dynamic co­
efficient of friction, v is the relative velocity between the two bodies in contact, and sgn(v) is the
signum function returning the sign of v. The abrupt transition in friction force between the two
branches results in a discontinuous model, which gives numerical issues when solving an ODE, as
the ODE becomes stiff.
28
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:39==
Ambrósio
The Ambrósio model, developed in 2003 [25], was designed to be more computationally robust.
It introduces a dead band around zero velocity, and each branch connects to the others, creating a
continuous function.
F = sgn(v)µdcdFn
cd =





(3.19)
where v0 and v1 are velocity tolerances and cd is a dynamic correction coefficient. The model only
accounts for the dynamic friction and omits stiction, rendering it less accurate at low velocities.
Nevertheless, the model is particularly suitable for scenarios dominated by sliding friction due to
high relative velocities, due to its numerical robustness.
Hyperbolic Smooth
The Coulomb can be smoothed using a multitude of functions, in this implementation the hyper­
bolic tangent function is used to replace the signum function, as done in [26].
F = Fd tanh
 v
vd

(3.20)
where vd is a velocity tolerance. The function is continuous and like Ambrósio, it does not model
stiction.
Benson
The Benson model was developed in 1990 [27] in an attempt to include the Stribeck effect.
F = Fnµ(v) sgn(v)
µ(v) = µd + (µs −µd) e−c|v|
(3.21)
where c is the exponential decay constant. The result is a more comprehensive model than the
classic Coulomb, yet it presents the same numerical difficulties due to the discontinuity of the
function.

in the commercial code MSCAdams. However, in the paper [29] a single equation was provided:

band. Fext is the resultant of external forces on the system and is often hard to estimate.
Dynamic Models
The static models introduced thus far does not model hysteretic effects, as these models have no
frictional memory. The following models are all dynamic models that capture different friction
phenomena by introducing one or more state variables.
Dahl
Dahl [31] came up with the idea to model the asperities as elastic bristles. The average deflection
of these bristles scaled by the bristle stiffness gives the friction force. Figure 3.3a illustrates a
conceptualization of these bristles and Figure 3.3b illustrates the mechanical model.
(a) Idea of bristle deflection
(b) Mechanical Model for Dahl
The average bristle deflection is denoted by the state variable z, the change in z over time is
modelled as:
˙z = v

1 −σ0z
Fd
sgn(v)
α
(3.24)
where Fd is the dynamic friction force, sigms0 is the bristle stiffness, v is the relative velocity of
the contacting bodies and alpha is an exponent that influences the stress­strain curve. The friction
force is then:
F = σ0z
(3.25)
This model is an advancement of the static models as it captures the frictional phenomena, pre­
sliding as well as a hysteretic effect. However, it does not model stiction nor does it model the
stribeck effect.
LuGre
The LuGre model was proposed by de Wit, Canudas et al. [32] and is an extension of the Dahl
model, by adding the capability of replicating stiction as well as the Stribeck effect. Just as is the
case with Dahl, LuGre utilizes the average bristle deflection. The model is illustrated in Figure
3.4.
30
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:41==
Figure 3.4: Mechanical Model for Dahl

v

where v is the relative velocity between the contacting surfaces and g(v) is a function describing
the Stribeck effect. Which could be parameterized as:
g (v) = Fd + (FS −Fd) e

)2
(3.27)
where Fs is the static friction force, Fd is dynamic friction force, and vs is the stribeck velocity.
The friction force from the bending of the bristles is given:
F = σ0z + σ1 ˙z + σ2v,
σi < 0
(3.28)
where, σ0, σ1, and σ2 are the coefficients of stiffness, damping, and viscosity, respectively. the
term σ2v accounts for viscous friction.
To use the LuGre model the parameters: σ0, σ1, σ2, FS, FC and vs need to be estimated, either
through experimental testing or using values found in the literature for similar applications [33].
Elasto­Plastic
The authors’ motivation for developing the Elasto­Plastic model [34] was that previous models,
based on Dahl’s bristle approach, had been unable to model both pre­sliding displacement and
stiction. It is based on the LuGre model and therefore uses the same mechanical representation as
depicted in Figure 3.4.
The friction force is given by the same equation as for the LuGre model, equation (3.28), with the

Gonthier
The Gonthier is another model based on the bristle deflection method, it was developed by Gonthier
et al. in [35]. It was developed to model more advanced frictional phenomena, while still ensuring
that it can be solved by explicit ODE solvers.
The friction force from the deflection of the bristles is given with a similar expression as the LuGre
model, however, without the viscous effect:
Fbr = σ0z + σ1 ˙z
(3.31)

The relative motion of the bristles must be exactly equal to the relative motion of the contacting
bodies, Hence, ˙zst = v.

Stiction is largely due to the interlocking of asperities and the molecular bonds formed between
them. The maximum force of stiction is denoted by Fs = µsFn. When a stick­slip transition
32
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:43==
occurs, these bonds break immediately. However, during a transition from slip to stick, when the
relative velocity v becomes zero, there is a delay before the asperities can interlock again and
reform the molecular bonds. This delay is referred to as dwell­time, results in a gradual increase
in the stiction force. To account for this behavior, the model introduces a new state variable:
˙sdw =
(
1
τdw (s −sdw) ;
s −sdw ≥0,
1
τbr (s −sdw) ;
s −sdw < 0,
(3.37)
where τdw is the dwell­time dynamics time constant and τbr is the bristle dynamics time constant.
The maximum friction force is then defined:
Fmax = Fn (µC + (µS −µC) sdw)
(3.38)
the bristle force should not exceed the maximum friction force, this is enforced with the saturation
function:
sat (Fbr, Fmax) =
(
Fbr;
|Fbr| ≤Fmax
Fbr
|Fbr|Fmax;
|Fbr| > Fmax
(3.39)
Reintroducing the viscous effect and using equation (3.39) to determine the maximum friction
force from the bristle deflection, the final expression for the friction force is:
F = −(sat (Fbr, Fmax) + σ2v) ,
(3.40)
Figure 3.5: Friction force as a function of relative velocity of the different friction models
Figure 3.5 depicts the friction force as a function of the relative velocity between two solid bodies
for various static models as well as the quasi­static version of the LuGre model. It is evident that the
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
33
==PAGE:44==
Coulomb model is a discontinuous function at v = 0, where it transitions between the dynamic and
static friction forces. Multiple attempts have been made to smooth and improve upon the Coulomb
function, each involving certain compromises. The Hyperbolic smooth model and the Ambrósio
model both neglect static friction. The Benson model includes the Stribeck effect. However, it too
is discontinuous. The velocity­based model is continuous and does include stiction and the Stribeck
effect, but it is offset from zero, resulting in a lack of accuracy at low velocities. The quasi­static
version of the LuGre model, assuming a constant relative velocity, looks similar to the Benson
model, while being a continuous function that also accounts for viscous effects, which leads to an
increase in friction force at higher relative velocities. It is noteworthy that all static models yield
identical results for relative velocities that are not near zero, i.e., if the relative velocity between
the rotor and stator is high, the different static models produce similar outcomes.
34
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:45==
4
Experimental Setup and Parameter
Estimation
The following chapter will be concerned with the experimental setup and parameter estimation.
4.1
Modifications to the Test­Rig
Although the focus of this master’s thesis has not been on improving the experimental test­setup,
some changes have still been made, these will be introduced in the following subsections.
4.1.1
Safety Modifications
Before work could commence on the test­rig it had to be brought up to the current safety standards
at DTU. This meant the construction of a new polycarbonate shielding around all spinning machine
elements and the installation of a safety stop. The machine drawings for the polycarbonate shield
can be found in Appendix C.5.
4.1.2
Implementation of new bushing material
It is of interest to do tests on the setup under different conditions to see how the simulation handles
different scenarios. It was deemed worthy to try a different commonly used bushing material with a
slightly harder surface than the brass, to see what influences that would do to the CoR. The chosen
material is bronze, more specifically the JM 3­15 from Johnson­metal. The machine drawing for
the bushing can be found in Appendix C.4.
4.1.3
Friction Force Test Setup
A separate form of testing on the test­rig, where the friction force can be investigated more isolated
was of interest. To apply enough load to the rotor without introducing a high breaking moment, as
the previous method using fishing line on the shaft [3], a new load applying setup has been devised.
The modified version of the removable sleeve, featuring a pulley on a roller bearing can be seen
in Figure 4.1, the machine drawings can be found in Appendix C.1.
Figure 4.1: CAD model of the friction setup assembly
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
35
==PAGE:46==
The purpose of the assembly is to allow for weight to be suspended at the end of the axle with
limited frictional resistance to the motor. Weights can be hung over the pulley using a steel wire
and can be incremented as needed. The PMB frame has been moved away effectively deactivating
the PMB, to have the rotor resting directly on the stator bushing. With the applied load and a
constant angular velocity of the motor, the system should acts like the 1­DOF model in Section
2.4.
4.1.4
Contact Estimation Method
In Section 5.1, there is a discussion on the issue of the inability to determine precisely when contact
occurs from the experimental data. Given that vibrations in the stator housing, induced by the
impact, persist after the rotor and stator have lost contact, it is evident that the current method
of calculating the normal force introduces inaccuracies. Therefore, it was considered worthwhile
to attempt to implement a method that can accurately determine the contact between the rotor
and stator and use this data to index the sensor data, disregarding forces present when there is no
contact. As the rotor is suspended by the PMB, and under normal conditions there is a gap between
the rotor and stator, with the housings made from polycarbonate, an open loop from the stator to
the spherical bearing housing will exist. However, once contact is made, i.e., the rotor touches
the stator, this loop is closed. To take advantage of this, a small current can be introduced using
a 9­volt battery, and the voltage can be measured using the DAU. The rotor effectively acts as a
large switch in the normally open configuration. The electrical diagram of the setup can be seen
in Figure 4.2
Figure 4.2: Electrical diagram of the contact detection implementation
The signal and the shield are bridged with a 10kΩresistor to reduce the current to under 1mA. The
negative terminal of the nine­volt battery is attached to the rotor through a mechanical connection
to the bearing housing using a ring terminal. The positive terminal is connected to the BNC cable.
The other wire in the BNC cable is attached to the stator, also using a ring terminal connection.
When there is no contact, the signal fed to the analog port on the DAU through the BNC cable will
be close to zero, and when contact occurs, it will spike to close to 9V .
4.2
Sensitivity of Force Transducers
During testing on the friction forces in Section 7.2 it was clear something was wrong with the
sensitivity of the force transducers. It must be mentioned that it is not ideal to use 5kN sensors to
measure forces of approximately 100N. However, the sensitivity can be adjusted to increase the
gain of the signal and thus getting a better resolution and Signal­to­Noise Ratio (SNR).
36
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:47==
From the calibration done in [1] it is clear that a quite low sensitivity was chosen, the scaling factor
from the linear fit is around 600N/V , which means that for forces in the range of around 200N, the
output voltage range is approximately 0.3V . The DAU can measure a voltage range of 10V , which
indicates that gain can be introduced to the signal to improve resolution. The resolution was even
worse in the friction testing done in [3], tried replicated in this thesis, where the applied forces were
significantly lower. As mentioned in Section 1.4, it is important to choose an appropriate sensitivity
for the sensors according to the load range used. This was supposed to be done in [1] as the authors
had the test­rig apart, allowing for a calibration of the sensors. The calibration was done in the
range of 20­50kg which is appropriate for the forces present during an impact when accounting
for pretension. However, the voltage range in these calibrations suggest that the sensitivity was set
lower than optimal. The calibration issue was detected too late in the process and since the test­rig
would need to be completely disassembled and reassembled and a new calibration rig would need
to be built to apply load to the sensors it was not possible to do a calibration within the time frame
of this thesis. However, the issues with the shifting use cases on the test­rig with different load
ranges inspired an idea to use the pretension bolts for calibrations. If a method to reliably calibrate
the sensors without disassembling the machine could be devised it would mean easy switching
of sensitivity. The idea is to use the tightening torque to calculate the pretension in the bolt and
thus how many kilos of force is applied to the sensor. The main concern is the large mechanical
advantage of the current bolts, makes it hard to obtain the torque measurement. A precise torque
measuring device that goes below 1Nm would be needed. Furthermore, it would be necessary to
be able to compare the calibration method with measurements from a standard calibration which
would require a disassemble of the test­rig, to validate the use of the mathematical model of the
pretension, such a model is introduced in [36].
Tt = P
d2
2
µ + cos θ tan β
cos θ −µ tan β

+ rnµn

(4.1)
Equation (4.1) relates the tightening torque Tt with the pretension P trough the mechanical advan­
tage and the friction in the threads and at the head or washer.
4.3
Reproducibility of the Test­Rig
To make any comparative analysis between the mathematical model and the results from the test­rig
it is imperative that the reproducibility of the test­rig is evaluated and quantified.
4.3.1
Kurtosis and Skewness
The first statistical test tools used are kurtosis and skewness metrics. These metrics are used to
verify that the data produced by the test­rig is normally distributed. Initially a large data set is
used, by concatenating the results from three tests of each 30 impacts the total number of impacts
in this data set is 90. The data used is from a normal hit with Ω= 0 and using the brass bushing.
The analysis of the kurtosis and skewness is done on the distribution of the magnitude of the initial
peak in the normal force data. A histogram of the distribution can be seen in figure 4.3.

4.3.2
Statistical Variance
Now that the assumption of a normal distribution is confirmed to be valid, provided that outliers
are excluded from the data, the reproducibility can be visualized using the mean and the Standard
Deviation (SD). The following data comes from a dataset where 30 impacts were conducted on the
brass bushing with angular velocity Ω= 0 and with magnet voltage and duration set to 45 V and
50 ms, respectively.
Figure 4.5: Spread of the magnet force data with brass bushing and Ω= 0
Figure 4.5 displays the statistical results for the magnet force data. The filled areas indicate the
spread of the data, with approximately 68% falling within the dark blue area and about 95% within
the light blue area. The blue line, representing the mean of the data, is utilized in the simulation.
As shown in the figure, the magnet data are quite consistent for the first 40 ms, but there is a
noticeable spread between 40 and 80 ms, which could lead to variations in the normal force data.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
39
==PAGE:50==
This variability might result from the spool not demagnetizing instantly after the signal is turned
off at 50 ms, thereby prolonging the force effect for approximately 90 ms instead of the specified
50 ms. The potential improvement of this issue using a H­bridge controller has been suggested in
[1].
Figure 4.6: Spread of the data for the normal force with brass bushing and Ω= 0
Figure 4.6 depicts the statistical spread of the normal force data. This shows that at least for a
simple horizontal impact with Ω= 0, the spread of the magnet force data does not translate into
an equally high spread in the normal force data and given the fact that the averaged magnet force
data is used in the simulation, making the actual force duration arbitrary, it was not deemed of high
importance to improve the repeatability of the solenoid magnet.
Figure 4.7: Spread of the data for the friction force with brass bushing and Ω= 0
Figure 4.7 depicts the statistical results for the friction force, which also shows a quite low spread
for most of the peaks.
4.4
Cross­Coupling Stiffness
It appears that some cross­couple stiffness is inducing a slight vertical movement of the rotor when
a horizontal pulling force from the magnet spool is applied. In this section the cross­coupling effect
is analysed with and without the coupling between the motor and the axle installed, to see if the
coupling is introducing most of this cross­coupling effect and if it is dependent on the angle of the
rotor. 10 tests have been conducted at each angle for both the full rotor system and for the system
without the coupling installed. The mean and SD of these tests have been calculated. Assuming
a normal distribution, it can be stated that the mean ± one SD encompasses approximately 68%
40
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:51==
of the data, while the mean ± two SD encompasses approximately 95%. In Figure 4.8 these are
represented by the filled areas.
Figure 4.8: Orbit plot for rotor angles from 0 to 2π in π
4 increments for tests with and without the
coupling installed
The figure shows the orbit for the rotor with the blue line representing the mean of the results of tests
with the coupling installed and the red line represents the mean of the results of the tests without
the coupling. The first thing that is clear is that the effect of the coupling is not as pronounced as
one could have expected. It also shows that the difference for the angles 90◦, 135◦, and 270◦, is so
small that it cannot confidently be concluded that any observed differences might not have been due
to chance. Furthermore, it does not seem like the coupling produces an increase in cross­coupling
for every angle. at 45◦it appears to decrease. The difference in the SD for each test does suggest
that the test­ rig sometimes are more accurate than others, as some outliers with significantly lower
pulling force may be produced during some of the tests and not for the others. This also seems to
be supported from the kurtosis analyse in section 4.3.1, that shows some outliers that challenges
the assumption of normal distribution. If kurtosis and skewness should be applied here to remove
outliers, more tests would need to be done for each angle. However, the results do show enough
of a difference to warrant a closer inspection of the couplings.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
41
==PAGE:52==
Figure 4.9: Maximum displacement in the vertical direction as a function of angles ranging from
0 to 2π in discrete increments of π
4 , for tests conducted with and without the coupling installed.
4.9 shows the maximum displacement in the vertical direction for the mean orbit with and without
coupling as a function of the rotor angle. The purpose of this plot is to determine whether there is a
cyclic cross­coupling effect resulting from the way the membrane is mounted inside the coupling,
using a total of four bolts arranged in two pairs, each pair offset by 90 degrees from the other. If
the membranes were tilted off axis it would lead to a cyclic cross­coupling.
The data does not show any cyclic behavior. However, it must be noted that the data with the
coupling is a superposition of multiple sources of cross­coupling that might be out of phase with
one another. With that said, the data with and without the coupling suggest an angle dependent
cross­coupling effect, which appears to not only come from the PMB, as it is currently modeled.
The spike at 135◦does imply that there might be an asymmetry in the coupling. It would be of
interest to do further tests with a new spherical bearing to see if any of the effect is coming from the
spherical bearing. Furthermore, a new coupling, possibly a high precision off the shelf diaphragm
coupling could be explored along with a realignment of the motor shaft to better coincide with the
center axis of the rotor.
4.5
Estimating the Coefficient of Restitution Experimentally
All the penalty method models used in this thesis depend on the Coefficient of Restitution (CoR)
for calculating the normal force. Previous attempts [2] to analytically calculate this metric, using
a formula derived by Kuwabara & Kono [37], produced values that were ultimately determined to
be too low and the CoR was then based on comparative results, assuming that the model will yield
more comparable results to the test­rig when the actual CoR is applied. To avoid this assumption
the CoR will be found empirically using the relative velocity of the rotor­stator pair before and
after an impact.
cr =
˙δ+
˙δ−
(4.8)
The CoR is estimated from averaged test­rig data of 30 horizontal impacts with an angular velocity
of Ω= 0, and with the magnet voltage and duration set to 45 V and 50 ms, respectively. First, the
rotor’s position data are differentiated to obtain its velocity in both the horizontal and vertical
direction. As no filter has previously been applied to the displacement sensors, a comparison of
a low­pass filter versus a smoothing algorithm was conducted. Both methods were applied to the
42
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:53==
position data prior to differentiation. The resulting rotor velocity from raw data, filtered data and
smoothed data in both directions can be seen in Figure 4.10.
Figure 4.10: Velocity of rotor in the horizontal and vertical direction ­ raw, filter and smooth
The sensors used on the test­rig have a max switching frequency of 400 Hz, which means that the
higher frequencies are not picked up by the sensors. With that consideration in mind, no further
information is lost using the low­pass filter, and it is thus preferred over the smoothing function.
It is also clear that the velocity in the horizontal direction is an order of magnitude higher than the
velocity in the vertical direction, which is expected from a horizontal pull.
Having analyzed the rotor data, attention now turns to the stator data. The filtered acceleration data
from the stator housing have been integrated to determine the velocity of the stator. To remove the
DC­level and thus ensure that the integration constant is zero, a high­pass filter was applied to the
acceleration data. The acceleration and velocity data for both directions can be seen in Figure 4.11.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
43
==PAGE:54==
Figure 4.11: Acceleration and velocity of stator in the horizontal and vertical direction
From Figure 4.11, it is evident that the velocity in the horizontal direction is higher than in the
vertical direction, consistent with a horizontal impact. Additionally, the velocity of the stator is
notably lower than the velocity of the rotor. The impact induces high frequency vibrations. How­
ever, the amplitude of these vibrations is very small. Given that the velocity of the stator is two
orders of magnitude lower than the velocity of the rotor, the stator has been assumed to be rigid
and is thus neglected in the calculation of the relative velocities used in Equation (4.8).
Finally, the velocity components are combined into a vector, consisting of a direction and a mag­
nitude, as depicted in Figure 4.12.
Figure 4.12: Rotor velocity vector magnitude and angle
Observing the angle over time, it becomes evident that the rotor is being pulled towards the stator
by the magnet until it abruptly changes direction at approximately 38 ms where it gets returned out
and away from the stator. Assuming that ˙δ−is represented by the peak velocity before the angle
changes sign and ˙δ+ is represented by the peak velocity after this point allows for an estimation of
the CoR. The estimated CoR values for the different bushing materials are presented in Table 4.3.
44
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:55==

Table 4.3: Overview of coefficient of restitution for different material pairs
The CoR values for the different bushing material are very close to each other, in other words,
changing the bushing material has little impact on the CoR. This similarity is more than likely due
to the similarity of the materials, given that even the PTFE bushing is mostly made of brass and
only a thin insert is PTFE. It may also be attributed to the relatively low impact velocity and the
fact that all three pairs are coupled with an aluminium sleeve on the rotor. Changing the sleeve
to a different material, such as steel, might have a more significant effect on the CoR. The results
presented in Table 4.3 can be incorporated into the simulation. Using a lower CoR than what was
previously utilized could affect the accuracy of certain normal force models, particularly the ’Hunt
and Crossley’ model as used in [3].
4.6
Fitting the Unbalance Response
The PMB has been disassembled and reassembled to allow for testing on the friction force, ex­
plained in Section 7.2. The orbit had also changed between the initial model fit of the unbalance
response performed in [1] and the experimental results in [3]. It is therefore of interest to refit the
orbit of the simulation to better reflect the current state of the test­rig.
(a) Experimental orbits for angular velocity of
17,17.2,17.4,19, and 20 Hz
(b) Linear fit of encoder angle for angular velocity of
17,17.2,17.4,19, and 20 Hz
Figure 4.13: Experimental orbit and angular velocity
Figure 4.13a depicts the unbalance response for five different angular velocities close to the first
natural frequency of the rotor. However, due to the residual unbalance being too great, it is not
possible to run the rotor between 17.4­19 Hz, as chosen on the controller, without hitting the stator
wall. The data does demonstrate more variety than previous results [1]. This variety in orbit over
time can be due to multiple reasons. The issue might stem from the motor delivering an uneven
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
45
==PAGE:56==
torque. However, the linear nature of the angle data from the encoder proves that that is not the
case. It might be due to increased play in the structure of the PMB, as it was disassembled and
reassembled by the workshop and some stretch in the bolts was observed. However, it was re­
tightened and appears completely solid. A more plausible cause is the play in the spherical bearing,
which allows for axial movement of the shaft and thus the PMB rotor, which could influence
the stiffnesses at the PMB. This issue has always been there but might have gotten worse due to
wear. Furthermore, the specific position of the PMB stator, after reassembly, might exacerbate the
problem. Wear on the bearing might have also slowly increased the play. Figure 4.13b illustrates
how the specified angular velocity in the motor controller is not actually the exact velocity of the
rotor. The data confirms that the unwrapped angular data from the encoder is completely linear. A
linear fit can be used to estimate the slope and thus the actual angular velocity. These values are
slightly lower than expected and can be found in Table 4.4. It is important to use the actual angular
velocity when fitting the orbit of the model.
