5
Model Analysis and Validation
5.1
Validating the Normal Force Models
It was originally planned to validate the normal force models by comparative analysis to an experi­
mental hysteresis loop. However, it is not a trivial task to estimate the indentation of the rotor­stator
pair on the test rig. Several factors affect the accuracy of the position data of the rotor. Firstly, the
relatively low switching frequency of the proximity sensors of 400 Hz means that the high frequen­
cies of the impact are unregistered. Secondly, the sensors measure the displacement indirectly as
they are mounted at the unbalanced disc, due to space constraint at the rotor­stator impact interface.
This implies that the rotor position at the impact interface must be extrapolated from the position
data at the unbalanced disc, which has been done by employing trigonometry and thus having a
linear relationship between the two positions. This is only accurate under the assumption that the
rotor is rigid and the bearing, acting as a pivot point, is fully constrained in translation. However,
as soon as the impact occurs all mode shapes are excited, and the assumption of rigidity falls apart.
Lastly, the maximum clearance measured by the position sensors is higher than the 2mm measured
with calipers. Possibly due to a combination of play in the spherical bearing and a slight bend in
the shaft.
Figure 5.1 depicts the experimental data, a fitted circle and the expected clearance. The experi­
mental data of the maximum clearance has been found by manually moving the rotor around the
inner wall of the stator bushing. The radius of the fitted circle is 2.17 mm and thus the sensors
predict a 170 µ larger clearance than it should.
Figure 5.1: Comparison of experimental and theoretical clearance
The issue of accurately determining the exact position of the rotor at the impact interface and
estimating the indentation experimentally was discussed in [2]. The authors of that bachelor project
attempted to use a high­speed camera for this estimation. However, the results from these tests did
not achieve the necessary accuracy. Additionally, since the scale of each pixel was not established,
the data could not be compared to the simulation without introducing an arbitrary scaling factor.
High­speed cameras are limited in resolution both spatially and temporally. Considering that the
entire impact duration can be as short as 3 ms, and the pixel size of the available camera is 20
µ, achieving the required resolution is challenging. This is particularly difficult when trying to
estimate an indentation simulated to be around 5 µ. For that reason, and because it introduces
challenges in synchronization with the existing sensors, it has been decided to abandon the idea of
using a high­speed camera.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
49
==PAGE:60==
It would be interesting to figure out where the discrepancy between the experimental and theoretical
clearance comes from and to upgrade the proximity sensors to have a higher switching frequency
to enable them to pick up the impact vibrations. However, it would still rely on a linear extrap­
olation to determine the position which could introduce inaccuracies. Due to time constraints on
the project, it was deemed infeasible to upgrade the proximity sensors on the test­rig. An alterna­
tive method is to introduce a small current to the rotor, through a slip ring or possible though the
spherical bearing and out through the stator detecting the signal as the rotor makes contact with the
stator. This is possible because the inner hosing is made of polycarbonate which works to isolate
the bushing from the rest of the structure. However, it does only work for conductive materials and
thus not for the PTFE bushing. This method has been further investigated and the documentation
of the implementation can be found in Section 4.1.4. However, as expected it is not without issues,
which is discussed in Section 7.1.2.
5.1.1
Comparative Analysis of Hysteresis Damping Ratio
The hysteresis damping ratio has been compared for all the models over the full range of CoR in
figure 5.2. Given the inability to estimate the indentation on the test rig, a numerical model from
Safaeifar and Farshidianfar [17] has been used as a reference. The numerical model was in that
paper calculated using a non­smooth approach. However, the data for the graph has been extracted
from a plot using the tool Plotdigitizer [38], where the points are manually placed on top of the
graph and the scales of the two axes are defined. The data will therefore have some amount of
error. However, it serves as a visual aid to show which models are most accurate at which values
of cr.
Figure 5.2: The hysteresis damping ratio versus the CoR compared to non­smooth numerical results
from Safaeifar and Farshidianfar [17] for all eleven contact force models
Figure 5.2 shows that the models: ’Hunt & Crossley’, ’Nikravesh’, ’Herbert & McWhannell’ and
’Lee & Wang’ are only valid for high CoR. Between those models it is the ’Herbert & McWhannell’
that stays accurate for longest with decreasing cr. However, the ’Hunt & Crossly’ is not far behind
and it is less computationally heavy. ’Zhiying & Qishao’ overshoots slightly for high values of
cr while it undershoots significantly for cr below 0.3. ’Gharib & Hurmuzlu’ overshoots quite
significantly for high cr and is only accurate for cr below 0.2 where it is among the most accurate.
The ’Hu & Guo’, and the ’Flores et. al.’ performs very similar, but are both shifted to the left
compared to the reference and thus tend to overestimate the hysteresis damping ratio for lower
50
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:61==
values of cr.
Figure 5.3: The hysteresis damping ratio versus the CoR compared to non­smooth numerical results
from Safaeifar and Farshidianfar [17] for full range models
This leaves ’Gonthier’ and the two new models, which have been implemented in this thesis,
’Safaeifar & Farshidianfar’[17] and ’Yu et. al.’ [18]. The models are both close to the reference.
However, the ’Yu et. al.’ seems to be more accurate in most of the range except for a slight bump
at cr = 0.25, probably as a result of the piece­wise function. The new models claims to be accurate
in the whole range of cr and also appears to be from figure 5.3. This means these models should
be able to model many different impacts. From this analysis the ’Yu et. al.’ should deliver the best
results as long as the CoR is away from cr = 0.25. ’Safaeifar’ should be better than ’Gonthier’ for
high values of CoR but fall short of it anywhere below cr = 0.6.
Based on the hysteresis versus CoR analysis the models can be put into three categories:
Soft Impact Models:
• GharibHurmuzlu
Hard Impact Models:
• HuntCrossley
• HerbertMcWhannell
• LeeWang
• Nikravesh
• ZhiyingQishao
• Flores
• HuGuo
Full Range Models:
• Gonthier
• Safaeifar
• Yu
The models in the hard impact category are expected to perform badly with softer impacts as the
hysteresis damping ratio is estimated far too low. However, at harder impacts they do perform quite
similarly to the full range models and to the non­smooth reference. The model in the soft impact
category is expected to perform badly for harder impact as it greatly overestimates the hysteresis
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
51
==PAGE:62==
damping ratio. Finally, the two new models and the ’Gonthier et. al.’ should be good at estimating
the hysteresis damping ratio, for both hard and soft impacts.
The simulation, described in chapter 2, was used to compute the normal force and indentation for
visualizing the hysteresis loop. The test rig has been employed to generate a horizontal pulling
force for simulating a hit with an angular velocity Ω= 0. The average magnet force data from
30 impacts was used in the simulation. Forty­four simulations were conducted, simulating each
of the eleven models with four levels of the CoR ranging from high to low energy dissipation.
The results of these simulations can be seen in Figure 5.4. Figure 5.4a displays the case with the
most energy dissipation. Most of the models are quite accurate in predicting the amount of energy
dissipation. However, several of the less computationally intensive models significantly under­
estimate the energy dissipation and, conversely, overestimate the indentation. In Figure 5.4b the
CoR is slightly increased, still resulting in an impact with notable energy dissipation. Interest­
ingly, the ’ZhiyingQishao’ model gets quite close to the more complex models, while the rest of
the less computationally intensive models continue to underestimate energy dissipation. Figure
5.4c depicts a more elastic impact. Here, the variability among the models reduces, however, the
’GharibHurmuzlu’ starts to show its limitations for modelling elastic impacts. Finally, Figure 5.4d
suggests that, for highly elastic impacts, the choice of model becomes almost arbitrary, as long as
one avoids the ’GharibHurmuzlu’.
(a) Cr = 0.15
(b) Cr = 0.3
(c) Cr = 0.6
(d) Cr = 0.9
Figure 5.4: Comparison of the hysteresis loop for all models at various CoR
Figure 5.5 displays the hysteresis loop for cr = 0.65, which was found in section 4.5, mirroring
the actual conditions in the test rig with the brass bushing, a scenario closely matched by the other
bushings. Observations from Figure 5.3 suggest that ’Yu et al.’ is fairly accurate, given how
close it is to the non­smooth numerical model. Comparing this to other models reveals that ’Hunt
& Crossley’, ’Nikravesh’, and ’Lee & Wang’ tend to underestimate hysteresis, while ’Gharib &
Hurmuzlu’ overestimates it, as expected. However, the differences among the remaining models

are not as big. Therefore, choosing the least computationally intensive model among these could be
advantageous. ’Herbert & McWhannel’ produces an almost identical hysteresis loop to ’Safaeifar’,
while the same can be said for ’HuGuo’ and ’Yu et. al’. It might therefore be interesting to see these
models compared with the experimental results in Section 7.1.4. The total simulation times for

6
Implementation of GUI
In the previous work done on the rotor­stator test­rig, the processing of the experimental data
was separate from the simulation. Furthermore, it was cumbersome to figure out where to edit the
different options for running the simulation and the isolation of the peaks was performed manually.
It was for that reason decided to implement a Graphical User Interface (GUI) to handle all the data
processing while giving a clear visualization of the options for running a simulation. The GUI has
been developed from scratch in this thesis while some of the supporting functions are modified
versions of script from [3] and [2], which are them­self revised scripts from [1]. The main GUI
code can be found in Appendix F.2. The revised data processing code can be found in Appendix
F.3, while the revised data calculation and extracting script can be found in Appendix F.4. All the
other scripts involved in the simulation that has been modified can be found in Appendix F.5.
6.1
Main GUI Window
The main screen of the GUI is displayed in Figure 6.1. It consists of an options panel on the left and
five panels on the right responsible for processing the data from LabVIEW, running the simulation,
loading previously saved data, plotting data, and providing feedback to the user, respectively.
Figure 6.1: Main interface of the GUI with color coded panels
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
55
==PAGE:66==
6.2
Options Panel
The left panel houses all the options related to the simulation. There are on/off toggles for activating
the individual machine elements and toggles for options related to the solver, like implementing
Rayleigh damping. Below the toggles are parameters that can be changed, such as the angular
velocity of the rotor Ωand the simulation length. Further down are the drop­down menus, allowing
for choosing normal force and friction force models as well as the material of the two impacting
surfaces. Finally, a button to automatically set the settings for an impact without rotational velocity
has been implemented alongside a reset button that resets the settings back to default.
6.2.1
Experimental Data Panel
The top panel on the right handles the data­processing of the experimental sensor data from the
LabView program. It takes in a TDMS file, which the user can choose by searching for the file on
the computer. A warning will be displayed if the file format is not TDMS.
Figure 6.2: Flow chart of experimental data panel
If the LabVIEW file is loaded correctly, the data will be processed and stored in a structure called
Sensors. The function handling the data processing is ’dataProcessingv5’ and it filters and shifts
the data as well as accounts for delays between sensors. The figure in the panel will update to show
the filtered force data from the force transducer installed behind the magnet spool. This data will
display peaks corresponding to each hit during a test, as demonstrated in Figure 6.1. Users can
adjust the settings for Min peak height (in newtons) and Min peak distance (in seconds) to ensure
that the function handling peak identification isolates only the main peaks and remains unaffected
by noise. These settings depend on the time between each hit and the magnitude of the noise.
Once all peaks associated with a hit have been marked with a red circle, and none of the smaller
peaks are marked, the experimental data for each hit can be extracted and stored. The function
responsible for calculating the experimental data from the filtered sensor data is ’extractingExper­
imentalData_v2.m’ and it is activated by the ’Crunch data’ button. It is important for the data to
follow a normal distribution if the mean of multiple hits is to be used. The assumption of normal
distribution is verified with a kurtosis and skewness analysis using the first peak in the calculated
normal force data for each hit. The data is presented in a separate panel, activated by the ’Crunch
data’ button, as a histogram with an overlay of the statistics, as seen in Figure 6.3. If the kurtosis
or skewness significantly deviates from a normal distribution, outliers may be removed to improve
the data quality.
56
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:67==
Figure 6.3: Histogram of first normal force peak distribution
The data in Figure 6.3 depicts normally distributed data after outliers have been removed. The
’Remove outliers’ button calculates the Interquartile Range (IQR) of the peak data and applies an
upper and lower bound defined as UB = Q3 + 1.5 · IQR and LB = Q1 −1.5 · IQR and removes
any hit data where the peak is outside of this range. Once the user is satisfied with the kurtosis and
skewness, the panel can be closed using the x in the corner and the experimental data can then be
saved as a .mat file for later use. The name of the file will automatically be the same as the TDMS
file, however, the user is free to rename it before saving.
6.2.2
Simulation Panel
The next panel below the experimental data panel is the simulation panel. It is quite simple; it
is from here that a simulation can be run once the desired settings have been applied. Once the
simulation is done it will update the name automatically using the key options as part of the name
and the user can save the file. It is again possible to change the name of the file.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
57
==PAGE:68==
Figure 6.4: Flow chart of simulation panel
Figure 6.4 illustrates the process of running a simulation. It verifies that the experimental data
is available for applying the magnet pull to the simulation, if not it prompts the user to process
or load some experimental data. Finally, it runs the simulation and saves all relevant data to a
structure called ’SimulationData’ the simulation data can then be saved by the user. A button to
run simulations for all normal force models has also been implemented to ease the simulation task
of comparing the normal force models.
6.2.3
Load Data Panel
The next panel in the column is the load data panel. This panel can be used to directly load ex­
perimental data and simulation data that has been run previously. With the data loaded in all the
plotting options become available. The automatic naming scheme for the simulation data can be
seen in figure 6.1.
Figure 6.5: Flow chart of load data panel
Figure 6.5 illustrates the data loading process, which is identical for both the experimental and
simulation data. Hence, the decision in the flow chart of whether to start with the experimental
data or not. Next, it verifies that the correct data structures are present in the data, if not the user
is prompted to load another file. If the required structures are present, the data is loaded, and the
user can choose to load more data or continue.
6.2.4
Plot Data Panel
The second to last panel in the right column is the plot data panel. Here multiple options for
plotting the data can be found. The Frequency Response Function (FRF), mode shapes, stability
map, Campbell diagram and discretization plots are all opened in a MATLABTM figure window.
However, the last three plotting options have their own panel with additional options for how to
structure the data. The stability map and Campbell diagram are remnant code from older imple­
mentation which were not fully implemented. However, due to them not being relevant during
the work on this thesis, they were left unfinished. However, they can be updated in future work if
modifications to the mathematical model are to happen.
58
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:69==
Orbit Plot
The orbit plot panel, as seen in Figure 6.6, consist of sliders for the start and end time for the orbit
data, so the plot can be adjusted to only show the relevant orbit data for both the experimental
results as well as the simulated results. It also has a reset button and the ability to give the plot a
name and save it. Finally, every panel that opens on top of the main GUI has an x in the corner to
close it.
Figure 6.6: Orbit plot ­ GUI
Contact forces Plot
The simulated normal and friction forces are compared to the experimentally obtained data in the
Forces panel, as seen in Figure 6.7. The data is aligned using the first peak in each data set. This
makes it easier to see that the peaks line up well even if the magnitude and width of each response
are different.

approximately 68 % of the data, and µ ± 2σ, representing approximately 95 % of the data. The
sliders can be used to adjust how much data after the first impact to include.

7
Results
The results of the three tests are presented in the following chapter. The non­rotating impact test
for normal force comparison, the friction test setup for friction model comparison and the final full
rotating impact test.
7.1
Normal Force Tests Results
The initial tests performed on the test­rig have been performed with no rotation of the rotor, i.e.,
Ω= 0. The purpose of these tests are to have an experimental result to compare the normal force
models from section 3.1.1 to, where friction does not play a significant role.
7.1.1
Experimental Setup
The test is conducted once for each bushing material, with each test consisting of 30 impacts.
The impact is initiated by the magnetic spool pulling on the ferrous sleeve with a 45 V supply for a
duration of 50 ms. This magnetic spool exerts a horizontal pull, resulting in a near­perfectly normal
impact, for proper comparative analysis of the normal force models. Prior to each test, the rotor
is aligned with markings to ensure a consistent orbit for each material. Given the 10 kHz cutoff
frequency of the signal conditioners, and using the Shannon­Nyquist criterion, a 20 kHz sampling
rate plus some oversampling to account for the filters roll off, would be adequate to prevent aliasing.
Nonetheless, due to the short duration of the impact events, the sample rate was increased to 48 kHz
to enhance the resolution of the transient response. The data from the DAU, as described in section
1.4, is processed using filters and techniques to refine and synchronize the signals. The raw data
from the force transducers has been calibrated using a linear fit from [5] to convert from voltage
to newtons, and then normalized to account for pretension. A fifth­order Butterworth high­pass
filter has been employed to remove low­frequency noise, and a fifth­order Butterworth low­pass
filter has been applied to remove high frequency noise. Different cutoff frequencies and filter
application methods have been explored to ensure that the data is not significantly altered, as there
was evidence of this happening with the prior filter settings.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
63
==PAGE:74==
Figure 7.1: A comparison of different cutoff frequencies and filter application methods for high­
pass filter
Figure 7.1 illustrates the difference between the previous high­pass filter using the MATLAB®
function filter() with a 100 Hz cutoff, and a new one using the function filtfilt() with a
10 Hz cutoff. It is clear that the filter() method adds a phase delay, and the excessively high
cutoff frequency seems to alter the data, while the new filter does not appear to shift the data.
Therefore, the new filter has been applied to the data. The same procedure has been performed for
the low­pass filter, trying different cutoff frequencies and filter application methods, which can be
seen in Figure 7.2.
Figure 7.2: A comparison of different cutoff frequencies and filter application methods for low­
pass filter
64
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:75==
From Figure 7.2 it is clear that the phase shift can again be eliminated by the use of the filtfilt()
function. It is also evident that the noise can be reduced by introducing a low­pass filter. Using a
cutoff frequency of 1500 Hz yields a very smooth signal. The reason for choosing this frequency
would that the data presented in Section 7.1.2 does not appear to show any significant vibrations
beyond this frequency. However, initially a 6 kHz cutoff was utilized for the four main transducers
in the housing using the filtfilt() function, while a 3 kHz cutoff was applied to the magnet force
transducer. The accelerometer data has also undergone calibration, with the same high­pass filter
applied; however, no low­pass filtering was applied. The position data was calibrated in [5] using a
sixth­order polynomial fit and normalized to the mean to correct for drift. No additional processing
was performed on the data. Finally, any delays between sensors were eliminated to synchronize
the measurements.
7.1.2
Experimental Results
The processed sensor data during an impact with Ω= 0 for the brass bushing, can be seen in Figure
7.3
Figure 7.3: Time series of sensor data during non­rotating impact for brass bushing
The figure demonstrates the synchronicity between the sensors, as both the accelerometers and
force transducers detect an impact simultaneously with the proximity sensors crossing the clear­
ance line. The vertical lines indicates when the contact signal boolean changes value. The region
between the lines represents the time span during which the contact signal indicates contact be­
tween the rotor and the stator and will be referred to as the contact region. The contact signal was
implemented to detect whether multiple impacts occur within the contact region, However, the
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
65
==PAGE:76==
contact signal might be too slow to react to the high frequency impacts. It is thus still not possible
to confidently say that the calculated normal force is appropriate for the entire contact region, as
some vibrations might be included where actual contact is absent. However, it can be used to con­
fidently determine when the contact region ends and thus removing the transient vibrations beyond
this point from the normal force. It is observed that the contact signal appears to interfere with the
position sensor data making the value rise during the detected impact region and fall again after
registering loss of contact. This is consistent with a DC level being introduced by the 9V contact
signal. The reason for this might be improper shielding between the outer mounting surface and
the signal of the proximity sensors, as the contact signal does introduce a small current in the entire
structure holding the proximity sensors. However, the other sensors do not appear to be influenced.
A possible solution to this might be to remake the mounting bracket in a non­conductive material
like polycarbonate.
Due to the low switching frequency of the proximity sensors, discussed in Section 4.5, no detailed
information about the impacts is visible in the position data. It is evident that a significant pulling
force from the magnet is present throughout the entire impact event, however, this is accounted for
by using the exact magnet force data to excite the digital twin. Similar plots for the impacts for
the other bushing materials can be found in Appendix D. It should be noted that the contact signal
cannot be used for the PTFE test, as it is not a conductive material. The data has thus instead been
synchronized using the position data and defining the contact region as when the magnitude of the
position vector exceeds the clearance, which yields quite similar results.
Figure 7.4: Normal force and magnet force for impact with Ω= 0 and brass bushing
Figure 7.4 depicts the mean value of the normal force and magnet force for the 30 impacts per­
formed using zero angular velocity and the brass bushing. The magnet force data does show good
repeatability for the first 40 ms, while having slightly more deviation between 40 and 80 ms. This
is a problem that has been discussed in [1]. However, it does not seem to translate into big devia­
tions on the force data. The SD is quite low for the first impacts, while there is a lot of spread in the
data for the last peak. This is due to differences in the contact signal boolean, the last peak is cut
differently in each hit. It might be argued that the end of the contact region is slightly misaligned
as it should not detect a loss in contact when the sensors give a large positive peak. This might
again be due to the inductance. It could therefore be reasoned that the last peak should be excluded
from the normal force data. Similarly, the data for the bronze bushing can be seen in Figure 7.5.
66
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:77==
Figure 7.5: Normal force and magnet force for impact with Ω= 0 and bronze bushing
The magnet force data depicts the same sort of spread as for the brass bushing, which is to be
expected, making the results comparable. The deviation in the normal force is also comparable.
The final results for the PTFE bushing is presented in Figure 7.6. Yet again the magnet data is
consistent with the other tests. The normal force data does not suffer from the same spread on the
last peak due to the contact signal not being used.
Figure 7.6: Normal force and magnet force for impact with Ω= 0 and PTFE bushing
Figure 7.7 depicts an overlay plot of the mean normal force for all three bushing materials. It shows
comparable results for all materials with a slightly more damped response for the PTFE bushing.
The PTFE results are also the cleanest, with only five peaks detected, while the results for the
brass and bronze test exhibits signs of high frequency vibrations giving smaller peaks throughout
the contact region. These peaks might not actually be relevant as there might be loss of contact.
However, it has not been possible to confirm this in this thesis, due to the inductance of the contact
signal. Given how different brass and PTFE material properties are, the small difference suggests
that the PTFE bushing is highly influenced by the brass outer structure holding the PTFE insert,
this is something to consider when modelling the PTFE bushing.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
67
==PAGE:78==
Figure 7.7: Comparison of normal force for different bushing material
The frequency spectrum can be inspected by running the time series through a Fast Fourier Trans­
form (FFT), which gives an overview of which frequencies are detected by the sensors. However,
the fast transient nature of the signal presents challenges with spectral leakage and noise.
Figure 7.8: Time series and frequency spectrum of the force transducers during an impact with
rotational velocity Ω= 0
on brass bushing
To make the FFT as reliable as possible multiple steps have been taken. Firstly, the data has been
windowed using a hanning window to reduce spectral leakage, significant spectral leakage will
still be present due to the transient nature of the signal. Zero padding has been used to increase
the resolution by decreasing the frequency bin width ∆f of the FFT, to make it easier to delineate
the different frequencies. Zero padding has been applied after windowing to not interfere with the
benefits of making the signal repeatable. Finally, the data is normalized by the mean to remove the
DC­level, removing the initial response at zero frequency.
The time domain and frequency spectrum for the force transducers during the brass testing can be
seen in Figure 7.8. For a normal impact without rotation, the SNR of the data from the north and

horizontal nature of the impact. For the east and west force transducers, the signal is stronger as
they are mounted in the horizontal direction. The sensors mainly show spikes around the natural

Similarly the time domain and frequency spectrum for the accelerometers can be found in Figure
7.9. The data shows peaks for higher frequencies, above the 1000 Hz upper bound of the model.
There are spikes at around 1.4 kHz 2.2 kHz, 4 kHz, and 4.2 kHz. Due to the high frequencies,
it is speculated that it is natural frequencies of surrounding structures, like the outer casing of
the impact house or the heavy frame which the test­rig is attached to, as these are stiff elements.
The test­rig has been attempted isolated from the frame through rubber mats, the main suspect is
therefore the outer casing of the impact house. Naturally, higher frequency modes will produce
higher accelerations for a given displacement, which in turn, according to newtons second law,
produces higher forces. Given that the model is valid in the frequency band of 0­1000 Hz it is
important to figure out where these higher frequencies come from and if they can preferably be
eliminated as they are causing discrepancies between the simulated and experimental normal force.
An FEM analysis of the outer casing of the impact house could be performed to determine if the
higher frequencies in the response are natural frequencies of the outer structure of the impact house.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
69
==PAGE:80==
The high amplitude of these frequencies and the fact that they are significantly more damped with
the PTFE bushing, suggest that they are important frequencies of the system and not electrical noise
from the DAS. Attempts could also be made to dampen the vibrations from the outer housing by
covering the outer shell with an automotive sound deadening material.
7.1.3
Simulated Results
The following section presents the results from the simulations at angular velocity Ω= 0. All
eleven contact models, presented in section 3.1.1, have been used for calculating the normal force
for the three different bushing materials. Subplots for each of the eleven models and 3 bushing
materials can be found in Appendix E.
Brass­Aluminium Contact Pair
The first contact pair to be presented is the aluminium sleeve against the brass bushing, which is
the one used in [1]. Figure 7.10 depicts an overlay plot of the normal force during the 4 ms of
the simulated impact between the surfaces in the brass­aluminium contact pair for all the eleven
models. Interestingly, the data show that, even though the comparison of hysteresis loop in Section
5.1 showed very small differences between ’Yu et. al.’ and ’Safaeifar’, these differences have a
significant impact on when the subsequent impacts occur. Given the similarity between ’Herbert
& McWhannell’ and ’Safaeifar’ and the fact that ’Herbert & McWhannell’ is significantly less
computationally intensive it will be used in the comparative analysis alongside ’Yu et. al.’ to
determine which model is closest to the experimental results.
Figure 7.10: Overlay plot of simulated normal force for Ω= 0 with the brass bushing
Bronze­Aluminium Contact Pair
Figure 7.10, depicting an overlay of the normal force for the bronze­aluminium contact pair, shows
a similar story, with slight differences between ’Herbert & McWhannell’ and ’Yu et. al.’.
70
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:81==
Figure 7.11: Overlay of results for the bronze bushing and Ω= 0
PTFE­Aluminium Contact Pair
Figure 7.12 shows an overlay of the simulated normal force for the PTFE­aluminium contact pair.
The elastic modulus of PTFE is significantly less than that of brass and bronze, which in turn makes
the generalized stiffness parameter lower, predicting lower forces. However, as seen in Figure 7.7
where the forces were quite similar, it does not make sense to use the material parameters of the
pure PTFE, as the bushing is a composite of brass and PTFE. Attempts were made to estimate
a equivalent elastic modulus. However, it was not possible to achieve comparable results. It is
therefore concluded that a different contact estimation not based on two spheres might have to be
used with equivalent stiffnesses for the two materials in the bushing.

7.1.4
Comparative Analysis
With the experimental results and all simulations for the normal force complete a comparative
analysis can be undertaken. The models chosen for this comparison are ’Yu et. al.’ and ’Herbert
& McWhannell’.
Figure 7.13: Comparison of orbit: Experiment vs. Simulation
Figure 7.13 depicts the difference between the initial conditions of the simulation and the experi­
mental data. This discrepancy does lead to differences in the location of the initial impact, which
could easily influence subsequent impacts. In the simulation the midpoint of the stator bushing is
assumed to coincide with the midpoint of the rotor at t = 0, while due to gravity and misalignment,
the actual rotor hangs down and a little towards the magnet side. These initial conditions might be
explored further in future work, trying to match the actual conditions.
Figure 7.14: Comparison of simulated and experimental normal force for brass bushing
Figure 7.14 depicts the time series of the normal force for the experimental data against the two
models for the brass bushing. From the data it is clear that even though the samplerate is high,
48 kHz, it is still not enough to capture the entirety of the peaks. This results in a lower magni­
tude than expected and could result in slightly skewed peaks. An example is the fourth peak, it
is skewed to the right, due to having too few samples to describe the whole peak. It is thus not a
stretch to imply that the actual peak might have been well aligned with the model data, although
with a different magnitude. The third simulated peak does not seem to align with the experimental
72
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:83==
data. However, the next two do seem to align quite well with ’Herbert & McWhannell’. The mod­
els predict a much longer contact event at the end lasting for over 1 ms, a contact phenomenon not
captured by the experimental data. This might be due to the initial conditions not being completely
similar between the test­rig and the simulation, as the simulation assumes a perfectly centered rotor
as the initial conditions. It could also be an issue with the experimental normal force, which is not
completely correct due to the incorrect sensitivity on the force transducers. Before concluding that
the model is not complex enough to predict the behavior of the test­rig, these issues should be ad­
dressed. Experimenting with using the initial conditions of the rotor, and implementing the effects
of gravity, might give a more accurate simulation, while a re­calibration of the force transducers
with a significantly higher sensitivity might lead to more accurate experimental results. Lastly, the
amplitude of each subsequent impact increases over time for the experimental results, something
that the model does not recreate, even though the actual magnet data is used in the simulation. An
explanation for this might be that the impacts excite all the modes leading to the higher frequency
modes giving higher spikes of normal force as the stator vibrates rabidly, this could explain why
the force increases over time and why the model does not seem to capture it. This could also be a
result of the initial conditions being different between the model and the experimental data.
Figure 7.15: Comparison of simulated and experimental normal force for bronze bushing
Figure 7.15 presents the results of the same comparison for the bronze bushing. The peaks are
aligned differently in this data, as the peaks are aligned at the first peak, which for the bronze
experimental data is slightly skewed. However, it does look like the ’Yu et. al.’ is better aligned
with the experimental data. The PTFE data has not been compared due to the discrepancy stemming
from the contact dynamics of the composite bushing being more involved to accurately calculate,
a more advanced model will therefore be needed to accurately estimate the contact dynamics of
the composite PTFE bushing.
7.1.5
Conclusion of Results
The main conclusion from the results of the normal impact testing is that discrepancies between
the experimental and simulated response is due to several factors pertaining to, insufficiency of the
model, and limitations of the test­rig and processing on the test­rig data. The testing of the filter
application has assured the accuracy of the filtered data and the presentation of the combined sensor
data demonstrated great synchronicity of the data. The shortcomings of the DAS are thus related
to the sensors them­self and the calibration and position of these sensors. It can be concluded
that the current implementation of the contact signal lag the required responsiveness to accurately
determine exactly when contact is occurring, and it presented issues with isolating the sensors
from the signal. However, it provided a reliable way to estimate the length of the contact region.
The position of the proximity sensors does present issues in determining the position of the rotor
at the contact interface, which makes it less reliable as a measure for contact. Furthermore, the
low switching frequency makes the sensors unsuited for detecting the impact. The statistic data
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
73
==PAGE:84==
confirmed the repeatability of the test­rig, and showed for a non­rotating impact how reliably the
same initial conditions can be induced. The FFT showed higher frequency modes with significant
amplitude in the acceleration data which influences the magnitude of the normal force. The origin
of these modes should be investigated and damped out, if possible, for a better match between
model and experiments. The data also showed that the difference between the different bushing
material is small.
The simulation results showed that multiple models were comparable, with ’Herbert & McWhan­
nell’ ultimately being the best choice. Furthermore, it showed that for the estimated CoR, the
previously used model ’Hunt & Crossley’ was not as accurate. The PTFE model did present the
issue of calculating the actual material parameters of the composite bushing, mostly made of brass
with a PTFE insert of 1 mm wall thickness and efforts is needed to accurately calculate the contact
dynamics of the composite PTFE bushing.
Lastly the comparative analysis showed that the initial conditions between the experimental data
and the simulations are different, which might also lead to discrepancies. Therefore, the initial
conditions of the model as well as introducing the effect of gravity in the model might be explored.
7.2
Friction Force Tests Results
The following section is concerned with the experimental test and simulations related to the friction
analysis.
7.2.1
Experimental Setup
The test­rig was modified with the changes explained in section 4.1.3. Figure 7.16 illustrates the
forces on the rotor as weight is applied to the pulley system. As the wire carrying the weight loops
over the pulley and is anchored down to the frame at approximately 180◦the load on the rotor
would be double the suspended mass. This load will be further amplified, due to the arm from the
pulley to the contact point. The Horizontal force balance is given:
−RA −2mg + RB = 0
(7.1)
and the moment balance around A is given:
L1RB −2mg(L1 + L2) = 0
(7.2)
where L1 is the distance from the spherical bearing to the contact point and L2 is the distance from
the applied load to the contact point. Solving these two equations yields the reaction force on the
bushing, RB. 3 kg was suspended on the axis using the load applying mechanism, which puts an
effective load on the rotor of approximately 72 N, for full calculations see Appendix F.1. 20 test
were performed for each material with a sample frequency of 48 kHz. The same data processing
was applied to the raw sensor data as explained in Section 7.1.1. Between each test the rotor was
sanded with 360 grid sandpaper to remove build up due to adhesion.
74

Figure 7.18a depicts the movement of the rotor, as measured at the unbalanced disc, during the
friction test when the rotor is started until it is turned off again. The plot illustrates that the data
has been successfully centered by fitting the clearance, using the manual clearance test before each
friction test, a crucial step to make the angle measurement reliable. Furthermore, it shows that the
unbalance of the disc is affecting the data. As the measurement is taken at the unbalance disc, these
oscillations are greater than they are at the actual contact interface. The unbalance also appear in
the data for the normal force and friction force in Figure 7.18b. The normal force data shows that
the stator was unloaded at the beginning of the test and then a spike appear in the data due to the
rotor being dropped on the stator, after which the rotor was manually moved around the stator
for 4­5 turn to get data on the clearance. At approximately 10 seconds the load was applied and at
approximately 25 seconds the motor was turned on for 5 seconds. These five seconds are the actual
test data used for calculating the friction coefficient. A low­pass filter was applied to remove any
vibrations from the data, while the unbalance is still present. Due to the issues with calibration
of the force transducers mentioned in Section 4.2 the force measurements are inaccurate. The
measured force is significantly lower than what is expected. However, due to the scaling being
equally off on the normal and friction forces, the calculated coefficient of friction is unaffected.
Figure 7.19 depicts the time series of the calculated angle θ illustrated in figure 2.8 along with the
frequency spectrum of that signal found using the FFT. The frequency spectrum shows that the
unbalance is dominating the response, and it has the same frequency as the angular velocity of the
rotor, as expected. Furthermore, the angular velocity of the rotor was limited to above 4 Hz due
to the motor only having enough torque at higher RPM. Gearing on the motor would allow for
slower speeds. However, it is not recommended to continue using the test­rig for friction model
validation, as it is too far from its intended use case.

7.2.3
Simulated Results
The results presented in this section are the predicted response of the one DOF friction model
presented in Section 2.4. The ODE was solved using the ’ode 45’ solver in MATLABTM, using the
experimentally found friction coefficient and angular velocity of the rotor. The relative velocity
between the contacting bodies is high for the entire friction test since the rotor is held at a constant
angular velocity by the motor controller. This has been simulated in the model by forcing the
angular velocity of the rotor to be constant. However, the side effect of this is that every single static
friction model will predict the same friction force and thus response. This is because the differences
in the friction models are how they handle the transition from static to dynamic friction around zero
relative velocity, a state that is never reached. The dynamic models are slightly different as they
are time dependent, with the LuGre model also implementing damping and viscous effects.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
77
==PAGE:88==
Figure 7.20: Comparison of simulated forces of brass bushing between Coulomb and LuGre
The forces for the static models, represented by the coulomb model and the dynamic model by
LuGre are presented in Figure 7.20. At the relatively low velocity of
v = 3.6Hz · 25mm · π ≈0.3m/s
(7.3)
the viscous effect is insignificant and it can be seen that the forces start at a similar value, and then
damping in the LuGre model eventually kills the response.
(a) Coulomb model
(b) LuGre model
Figure 7.21: Simulated response of brass bushing for a static and dynamic model
Figure 7.21 portrays the angle θ and angular velocity ˙θ of the rotors trajectory along the stator wall
for Coulomb and LuGre respectively. Figure 7.21b and Figure 7.21a shows the oscillations around
the equilibrium between the restoring force from gravity and the pulling force from friction, while
only the LuGre model dampens the response over time. With the recommended parameters in
the LuGre model, the oscillations appear to be slow to die out, at approximately 10 seconds. The
LuGre predicts that the rotor will stop oscillating like a pendulum and find its equilibrium where
it will slide in place at approximately 55◦, where the static models have no way of dissipating
energy and they predict infinite oscillations. Both figures also show the frequency spectrum to
78
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:89==
demonstrate at which frequency these oscillations occur. The models predicts large oscillations at
approximately 2.43 Hz and 2.44 Hz respectively and a high equilibrium around 55◦.
7.2.4
Comparative Analysis
The original plan was to use the data from the friction tests to compare the movement of the rotor
to the one DOF model in Section 2.4.
The one DOF friction model made in Section 2.4 was intended to provide a way to validate the
various friction models with testing under conditions more similar to the rotor impact event. How­
ever, as demonstrated in Figure 7.19, the data does not show any notable oscillation around the
equilibrium between the restoring moment from gravity and the pulling moment from the friction
as predicted by the simulation. Although simple models can sometimes model complex events
with quite good accuracy, there are instances where the assumptions used for the simple model ig­
nore important aspects of the actual events. This leads to significant discrepancies between model
predictions and reality. This is one such example. The assumption that the rotor is a fixed radius
from the center of the stator, and thus always in contact with the stator wall, was what facilitated
a one DOF model. However, it might be the reason that the model predicts significantly larger
oscillations and a higher equilibrium than observed. The voltage from the contact signal does not
drop down to zero. However, it does have significant voltage fluctuations when the rotor is on,
which could support the theory of the vibrations from the spinning rotor leading to intermittent
loss of contact. This loss of contact would explain why the rotor only moves approximately 15◦
up the stator wall, as the restoring force from gravity is constantly doing work, while the friction
force is intermittent. The framework is there to give the model free movement in the radial direc­
tion, as it has been made in three­dimensional vector space. Another issue with the comparison
is the unbalance of the rotor. It has been ignored in the model, while the experimental results are
measured at the unbalance disc, where the effect of the unbalance is amplified. One could remove
the unbalance from the experimental data by subtracting the sinusoidal signal corresponding to the
unbalance from the response. However, this step was not performed as it was concluded that a lot
of additional work was to be done on the model to make the results comparable, with limited return
on investment given the similarity of the model results under these conditions. The rotor finds the
equilibrium very fast, due to the oscillations being significantly smaller than predicted and per­
haps significant damping not probably simulated. For proper comparison of the friction models
around zero relative velocity would require a dedicated test­rig. Inspiration could be drawn from
the design in [39] showing the principle of stick slip.
7.2.5
Conclusion of Results
This section summarizes the results from the tests and simulations related to the friction force, and
presents the conclusions made from these results.
The experimental results were used to get data on the coefficient of friction of the actual material
pairs in the rotor stator test­rig. However, it was a difficult test to perform on the test­rig, as the
test­rig was not made for continuous wear. Adhesion effects during friction affected the surface on
the rotor, making it rougher over time. The rotor sleeve was sanded between each test to reduce
the effect of this. However, it was not possible to do the same to the stator bushing. The data
was found to be normally distributed and the mean value for the friction coefficient could be used.
However, due to the excessive wear on the PTFE bushing cutting the experiment short, the results
for that bushing are subject to uncertainty. The tests also revealed a discrepancy between the
angular velocity specified at the motor controller and the one measured at the encoder. This is
something to be aware of for future testing, to allow for correct comparison to the simulations.
The issues with the calibration of the force transducers mentioned in Section 4.2 also meant that
the magnitude of the force is measured too low, and cannot be compared to the simulation.
The simulation results revealed that, for the use case of a spinning rotor hitting a stationary bushing,
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
79
==PAGE:90==
there were no difference between the static models, as the motor kept the relative velocity of the
impacting bodies at a level far from zero. It also revealed that for the recommended parameters in
the dynamic LuGre model, the damping was quite slow to kill the response. Which could suggest
that the difference between the static and dynamic models for a fast impact event might not be
so big. Furthermore, it was found that the friction experiment used in this thesis could not be
modelled with only one DOF as it greatly over predicted the amplitude of the response and the
moment balance equilibrium.
The overall conclusion for the results presented in this section must be that the use of the test­rig
to compare models is not the right option and a new setup should be made if comparison of the
friction models around zero velocity is to be performed. However, for the impact testing with a
spinning rotor and a stationary bushing the simplest static model would be sufficient for calculating
the friction force. The dynamic models might give slightly better results. However, the parameters
are hard to estimate, and the viscous effects are not significant at the relative velocities present in
the operating range of the test setup.
7.3
Full Rotating Impact Tests Results
With the work on validating the different models and estimating the different parameters complete,
a comparative analysis of the full rotor stator impact setup, with Ω̸= 0, can be performed. The
forces will be examined and compared with the simulations and conclusions will be made on the
state of the experimental test setup and the corresponding mathematical model.
7.3.1
Experimental Setup
The chosen angular velocity on the motor controller, for these experiments is 17 Hz, a value that
has been chosen to keep it comparable to previous results in [1], [2] and [3]. The DAS is initiated
once steady state conditions are reached and the impact is initiated by the magnetic spool pulling
on the ferrous sleeve with a 35 V supply for a duration of 50 ms. These values are chosen to match
the original conditions in [1]. However, an oversight in the previous work has been to document
the distance from the magnetic spool to the ferrous sleeve. As the test setup was taken apart to
borrow the force transducer it was reassembled at the beginning of this thesis, without knowledge
of the previously used distance. Given that the magnetic force is dependent on the distance to the
sleeve, it is an equally important measure as the magnet voltage supply and signal duration. It was
therefore decided to use a feeler gauge set to specify the distance between the magnet spool and
the sleeve to make it easier for future work to replicate the results. Multiple distances were tried
and a good gap was chosen to be 1mm. For these tests to reproduce a repeatable result the magnet
activation has been synchronized using the angle data from the encoder and steady state is reached
between each activation. Many tests were performed to diagnose an issue with the unbalance
response varying excessively between hits. It was observed that the unbalance response would
increase over time during the test. It would stay stable if 20 new hits were performed right after,
However introducing a break between tests would bring the orbit back to a response comparable
with the one done in the orbit fit, which would again increase over time. The suspected reason for
this is a heat buildup in the bearing, which through different mechanisms could change the balance
of the rotor. This is supported by the sounds made by the bearing, which are related to friction.
A new bearing is most definitely needed, and it should be maintained using grease to preserve the
tight tolerances. Therefore, the data used in the following section has a higher unbalance response
than the fitted data, which was is not affected by the heating problem.
7.3.2
Experimental Results
Figure 7.22 depicts the orbit of the rotor during an excitation for four different angles, chosen to
replicating the results in [1]. The results shows less repeatability, which is consistent with the
orbits presented in section 4.6. Wear of the spherical bearing is suspected to be the cause. More
80

Figure 7.22: Experimental orbit at the unbalance disc for four different angles.
importantly, the angle does not match up with the results in [1]. The suspected reason for this is that
there at some point has been slip or a disassemble of the rotary encoder and it might therefore not
align with the previous zero point, causing a shift in the rotor’s position in its orbit at zero­degree
angle. However, the zero angle on the encoder does line up with markings on the rotor, when the
markings are on the top of the rotor the angle is zero. It has not been possible to find documentation
for the markings on the rotor, and it is thus not known when these were implemented. However, it
can be used for future reference. It would also be consistent with the issue of the discrepancies of
the hit angle between the experimental results and the simulation explained in [3]. While the results
are dissimilar with the original work, it does appear to be close to a 90­degrees shift. Making the
90­degree angle comparable to the previous 180­degree angle, with a direct path to the bushing
wall. This angle is therefore chosen for model comparison. The orbit fit performed in Section 4.6
should take care of the bulk of the discrepancy between the experimental excitation angle and the
simulation angle, as the unbalance will be defined using the correct reference for zero angle. There
is still a slight delay between when the magnet is activated and when it actually starts to pull, that

Figure 7.23: Orbit data for 20 impacts at 90◦on brass bushing
The magnet data has been extracted for each impact by finding the peaks and going backwards until

events as evident by the low SD of the magnet force up until the impact region. Applying these
indices to identify the angle when the magnet spool is actually activated, shows quite a significant
spread in the data as can be seen in the box­plot in Figure 7.24. The discrepancy between the
specified angle and the mean magnet activation angle is due to a delay on the test­rig which was
documented in [1]. However, it does appear like this delay is not constant given that the Labview
program activates the magnet within a tolerance of ± 1­degrees but the SD for the measured angle
is 16.4 degrees. The source of the varied delay should be further investigated.

Figure 7.25: Normal, friction, and magnet forces for impact with Ω= 16.75 Hz and brass bushing
Figure 7.25 presents the spread in the data for the normal, friction and magnet forces for 20 impacts
at Ω= 16.75 Hz. The data shows an increase in spread compared to the same results for a normal
impact event with Ω= 0 showed in Figure 7.4. An expected outcome given the spread in the
magnet activation angle and the orbital position. It would be of great interest to find the sources of
this variability before a proper comparison to the model can be performed. An FFT of the magnet
data was performed to identify the frequencies in the increased noise that was observed. Figure
7.26 shows the frequency spectrum for the magnet data. The data demonstrates peaks at 50, 150
and 300 Hz, which is consistent with interference from the power supply for the motor, which
alternates at 50 Hz. There might therefore be an issue with isolating the signals from the power
supply.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
83
==PAGE:94==
Figure 7.26: Frequency spectrum of magnet force data
Furthermore, as previously discussed, force data is registered when there is no contact, as evident
by the positive values for the friction force at times where the normal force is zero, which is non­
physical. This is due to the way the data is processed, and the lag of a proper contact detection is
the root problem. Similarly, the same data can be seen for the bronze bushing in Figure 7.27. The
data for the PTFE bushing was not done because the issue of estimating the material parameters of
the composite bushing was not resolved in time. It is therefore not possible to compare the results.
Some more work should be put into working out how to model the contact between this composite
bushing and the aluminium shaft, using the constitutive equations in section 3.1.1, as the Hertzian
theory with two spheres might be insufficient.

Figure 7.28: Simulated normal and friction forces for brass and bronze bushing
Figure 7.28 depicts the simulated forces for the brass and bronze bushing. The data shows quite a
significant normal force despite the low pulling force of the magnet. Furthermore, the data does
show that the slight change in CoR has translated to a slightly longer duration between each impact
as more of the energy is preserved, making the rotor bounce further out. However, the magnitude
of the peaks is quite similar.
7.3.4
Comparative Analysis
With the experimental data and simulated data processed the only thing remaining is to compare
them. The experimental data showed significant variation due to the state of the test­rig which
makes it difficult to compare the data. The low repeatability of the orbit, and the varying magnet
activation angle, coupled with the increase in the unbalance response due to the heating effect, has
caused a significant discrepancy between the experimental orbit and the simulated orbit.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
85
==PAGE:96==
(a) Brass bushing
(b) Bronze bushing
Figure 7.29: Comparison of orbit between experimental data and simulated data
Figure 7.29 depicts the orbit comparison of the experimental mean to the simulated response for
17 Hz and 16.75 Hz, respectively. Figure 7.29a is for the brass bushing and Figure 7.29b is for
the bronze bushing. Both plots shows a closer fitting orbit for the 17 Hz. This is likely due to the
influence it has on the unbalance response, which has been hard to fit due to the changing nature of
it. Even though the results are worse with the 16.75 Hz, it is speculated that is coincidental and that
the problems lie elsewhere, due to the compelling evidence from the rotary encoder, suggesting the
angular velocity is 16.75 Hz. However, it is worth noting. The difference in the mean trajectory,
between the two sets of tests, illustrates the variability of the orbit. Overall, the experimental data
is not in a state for useful comparison with the digital twin.
7.3.5
Conclusion of Results
The main conclusion from the results of the full impact tests is that there still is too many uncertain­
ties related to the test­rig and DAS for an accurate comparison to the digital twin. The fundamental
issues are the uneven orbit and the unstable magnet activation as it relates to the timing and the
magnitude of the pulling force. However, other things have been confirmed for future work. The
zero angle of the experimental setup is aligned when the markings on the rotor are on top, and the
fit has aligned the simulated orbit to the same zero reference. The angular velocity of the rotor is
held constant by the motor controller while the actual velocity is slightly less than the controller
value. Furthermore, it was concluded that there is work to be done in order to make the forces
comparable to the model by identifying when contact occur, as the current results predicts friction
forces when the normal force is zero due to the transient vibrations after each hit counting towards
the normal and friction forces.
86
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:97==
8
Conclusion
In the present work, the dynamics of the rotor stator test­rig introduced in [4], has been further
investigated. A series of experimental test has been performed in order to fully understand the
shortcomings of the test­rig as well as lead to insights into what could be done in the simulation.
Rework on the test­rig was not of concern in this project. However, some changes where made to
it: it was outfitted with a safety cage around the spinning rotor and a safety stop was put in. A new
bushing was implemented to model a different material. The chosen material was bronze, as it is
a commonly used bushing material with a slightly harder surface than the brass. A new contact
signal was implemented for the detection of contact between the rotor and the stator, it was found
to not be able to detect the fast impact events inside the contact region, while giving a good idea
of when it ends. Unfortunately, it was found to cause issues with the proximity sensors, due to the
mounting surface not being isolated properly. A new sleeve was developed to apply load to the
rotor without introducing excess breaking moment on the rotor. This was used during the friction
tests and it worked well. However, due to the sensitivity of the force transducers being too low it
was not possible to accurately measure this force. The force transducers were found to produce
wrong results. A calibration was not feasible to get done as a complete disassembly of the impact
housing is required to reach the sensors. An idea was presented to make a setup for calibrating the
sensors while installed in the test­rig using the pretension bolts, which would allow the sensitivity
to be adjusted for different test requirements. However, there might be several pitfalls making such
a solution infeasible, such as the low moment that would need to be applied and measured with high
accuracy. Test were performed to identify the source of the cross­coupling effect. A comparison
with and without the coupling was performed. The data did not show a cyclic result. However, the
resolution was low with tests performed for every 45 Degrees of rotation. The coupling did seem
to introduce a worsening of the cross­coupling effect for most angles with high relative increase
at 135◦. The experimental orbit has been found to be sensitive to small changes on the setup. It
was fitted to account for the new unbalance response due to disassembly of the test­rig, as well as
the slip of the rotary encoder, where it was observed that the actual angular velocity was slightly
lower than the controller value.
The coefficient of restitution was estimated using the average velocity of the rotor during an impact
with Ω= 0 as the velocity of the stator was found to be negligible. The CoR were found to be
quite similar for the three materials, which is likely due to the bushing materials being so similar
and due to the low relative velocity of the impact. Introducing a steel sleeve would make for an
interesting comparison with a possibly bigger difference.
The digital twin was left more or less untouched. However, new contact models were introduced,
as newer models had been developed that promised accurate results in the entire range of CoR.
The normal force models where validated using a numerical method from Safaeifar as it was not
possible to measure an experimental indentation. The Hertzian contact theory based on two spheres
which has been used was concluded to perhaps not be suffice for the composite bushing with
the PTFE insert, as it was not possible to calculate equivalent material parameters that yielded
comparable results with the experimental data. The normal impact test results showed that the
discrepancies in the experimental and simulated results where due to several factors, related to the
DAS and the simulation. The higher frequency modes, the lag of gravity and the different initial
conditions are all shortcomings of the model, while the low switching frequency of the proximity
sensors, the wrongly calibrated force transducers and the lag of proper contact detection are the
main shortcomings of the DAS. It was concluded that for the CoR used in the project that the
’Herbert & McWhannell’ was the most accurate model.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
87
==PAGE:98==
For validating the friction force models, a new one DOF model was derived to model a friction
scenario where the rotor acts like a rolling pendulum with sliding included. The model was made
in 3D vector space to make it simpler to upgrade in the future, it was based on Euler’s second law of
motion to include the inertia of the rotor which was deemed important. The model was developed
prior to the friction testing due to delays in the workshop. It was later found that the model did
not properly represent the dynamics of the test­rig. It was concluded that the rotor had movement
in the radial direction, leading to loss of contact and thus a significantly lower response, which
never seemed to cross the equilibrium, making it difficult to compare the different models with the
experimental results. It was concluded that a dedicated test­rig for the friction force around zero
velocity should be developed if the models are to be compared, akin to the one described in [39]
for showing the principle of stick slip. However, for a rotating impact the best option was to use
a simple static model like the ’Ambrosio’, given the high relative velocity. An estimation for the
friction coefficient was obtained, however, it would also benefit for a dedicated model where the
materials can be switched out with new ones between each test, to eliminate the influence of wear.
The rotating impact test revealed a significant reduction in repeatability of the test­rig. Some of the
variance can be explained by wear on the bearing and some must be related to the electronics, given
the variance in the magnet activation angle. Some electrical noise from the AC power supply on
the motor was also observed in the magnet data. The amplitude of these frequencies had increased
compared to previous data, making it an area of concern. The orbit fit data revealed a discrepancy
between the motor controller value and the actual angular velocity. From each of the observations
made in this thesis it is clear that the test­rig needs to be overhauled before further comparison to
the digital twin.
As part of the project the scripts have been thoroughly analyzed and joined together into a cohe­
sive appdesigner GUI for making it easy to run simulations with different models in the future.
A load function was implemented to quickly identify the multiple impact events in a LabView
file, checking for normality using the kurtosis and skewness metrics, excluding outliers based on
the interquartile range, and calculating the mean data. The main data processing script was also
overhauled for better filtering to preserve the trend of the data and avoid phase shift. The options
panel was implemented to make it significantly easier to change the parameters of the model, as to
avoid mistakes. The data was structured in structures to be saved to a MATLAB data file. Some
plotting options were implemented to quickly asses the data. However, using the saved structures
one can make whichever representation of the data as one would like. The LabView program was
also updated to fit the new contact signal. A block diagram was made for documenting the test­rig
DAS in its current configuration as some of the sensors on the test­rig had been changed.
8.1
Future Work
The conclusion on the entire body of work in this master’s thesis has revealed a significant number
of concerns regarding the mechanical state of the test­rig. These issues should be resolved before
any further modifications to the digital twin is performed, to avoid modeling elements that could
be eliminated with proper mechanical design. Recommendations for future work is provided in
this section.
8.1.1
New Bearing
The spherical bearing on the test­rig has been suspected of causing multiple issues on the test­
rig. Some of these issues are as a result of the type of bearing used, i.e. the axial play and the
an­isotropic damping. While new issues appears to have risen due to wear of the bearing. As
mentioned in [1] it might be useful to substitute the spherical bearing for an ordinary ball­bearing
mounted to a sheet metal membrane. The sheet metal could in theory allow for small pivot angles
while eliminating axial play. It would also be important to make sure the bearing is well maintained
to reduce wear in the future.
88
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:99==
8.1.2
New Coupling
The cross coupling analysis performed in this thesis suggest an issue with the couplings on the
test­rig. The membranes might be the culprit. Ordering precision diaphragm couplings, of the
shelf, could be a solution. In addition, the alignment of the motor shaft to the spherical bearing
could possibly be improved, by giving more adjustment options.
8.1.3
DAS
A series of changes are recommended for the DAS. Issues related to noise from the outlet to the
motor would need to be investigated. Currently, the wiring layout lack organization, with wires
carrying high voltage running in the same tracks as sensitive signal wires. Implementing cable
management to ensure the isolation of each signal, avoiding interference, would be beneficial. To
be able to utilize the newly introduced contact signal, the proximity sensors should be isolated from
the frame. The proximity sensors would also need to be upgraded to sensors with faster switching
frequency, allowing for registration of the crucial frequencies of the impact events. These would
probably need new mounting brackets. It would make sense to redo the mounting in polycarbonate
to make sure they are isolated. The magnet activation also appear to have issues. The MOSFET
transistor used for the activation could be replaced by a motor controller with integrated H­bridge,
allowing for demagnetization of the spool giving more control over the magnet duration. The
wiring should also be checked. Finally, the force transducers would need re­calibration, alterna­
tively sensors with much lower nominal rating could be used, as the current configuration of the
test­rig does not exceed 500 N. If the sensors are preserved, the idea of making a calibration method
with the pretension bolts could be explored. It is however, not a method that is guarantied to yield
success.
8.1.4
Identifying the High Frequency Vibrations
The spectral analysis revealed high frequencies from the accelerometers, which directly affects the
normal force due to the relationship between force and acceleration in newtons second law. The
source of these vibrations are suspected to be the outer casing of the impact house. This could be
substantiated by doing a FEM analysis on the outer casing, identifying the natural frequencies. The
structure could also be attempted damped, for example with the use of automotive sound deadening
material.
8.1.5
Contact Dynamics
When the test­rig has been brought up to a state where it produces repeatable results, focus could
return to refining the digital twin. Trying a new sleeve material would be of interest to get a larger
difference in the CoR between different impacts. A steel sleeve would be recommended, and a
PTFE sleeve could also be explored. Furthermore, developing a model that can account for the
composite material in the PTFE bushing, to have comparable results to the experimental data,
would be of interest.
8.1.6
Friction Model Comparison
If the friction models are to be used in a different scenario in the future, with much lower relative
velocities between the contacting surfaces, it might be more relevant to compare the models around
zero velocity. The results from the work done in this thesis has shown that a new test­rig would
need to be designed. Such a test­rig should be able to demonstrate stick slip. The article [39] is
a good starting point for how such a device should work, it has been replicated in many studies
since.
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics

level.

restart:
m:=3*Unit('kg')
m d 3 kg
g:=9.82*Unit('m/s^2')
g d 9.82 m
s2
l__1:=426*Unit('mm')
l1 d 426 mm
l__2:=95*Unit('mm')
l2 d 95 mm
l1:=-R__A-2*m*g+R__B=0
l1 d K58.92 m
s2  kg K RAC RB = 0
l2:=l__1*R__B-2*m*g*(l__1+l__2)=0
l2 d 426 RB mm K 30697.32 kg m
s2  mm = 0
sol:=solve({l1,l2},{R__A,R__B})
sol d RA = 13.13943662 N, RB = 72.05943662 N

str to boolean 1/0
181
if strcmp(value,'On')
182
binaryValue = 1;
183
else
184
binaryValue = 0;

189
function update(app) % runs every time something is changed in the GUI
190
191
app.ME = machineelement_location_fun(app.parameters.Ne,app.ME); %
calculates machine element location based on options chosen
192
buildingGlobalMatrices(app); % calculates the matrices
193
solvingEVP(app);
194
195
if strcmp(app.ImpactSwitch.Value,'Off')
196
app.EVPDropDown.Items = {'general','standard'};
197
else
198
app.EVPDropDown.Items = {'general'};
199
end
200
assignin('base','parameters',app.parameters);
201
assignin('base','ME',app.ME);
202
assignin('base','MAT',app.MAT);
203
assignin('base','EVP',app.EVP);
204
assignin('base','SimulationData',app.SimulationData);
205
assignin('base','ExperimentalData',app.ExperimentalData);
206
assignin('base','Sensors',app.Sensors);
207
end

function DOF = calculateDOF(app) % calculate DOF based on number of
elements
210
DOF = (app.parameters.Ne+1)*(app.parameters.NEdof/2);
211
end

213
function [Omega,Omegarpm] = calculateOmega(app) % Converts Omega from Hz
to rad/s
214
Omega = app.parameters.Omegahz *2*pi;
% Angular velocity [rad/s
]
215
Omegarpm = Omega*60/2/pi;
% Angular velocity [rpm]
216
217
end

function postProccessing(app) % runs eventDetection and
reralculatingImpactData
220
app.eventDetecter();
221
app.recalculatingImpactData();
222
end

224
function [E, nu,radius,rho] = materialParameters(~, chosenMaterial) %
saves parameters based on materials chosen
225
226
if strcmp(chosenMaterial ,'Brass')
227
E = 100e9;
228
rho = 8400; % Density of brass
229
nu = 0.35;
230
radius = -0.029/2;
231
elseif strcmp(chosenMaterial ,'Bronze')
232
E = 100e9;
233
rho = 8800;
234
nu = 0.3;
235
radius = -0.029/2;
236
elseif strcmp(chosenMaterial ,'PTFE') % Should be changed , as the
volume fraction is not the correct approach to model the PTFE/
Brass composit bushing
237
E_brass = 100e9;
238
E_PTFE = 1.6e9;
140
Friction and Normal Force Models for Nonlinear Rotor­Stator Contact Dynamics
==PAGE:151==
239
ratio = 0.615; % volume fraction of PTFE
240
E = E_PTFE*ratio+E_brass*(1-ratio);
241
rho = 2200; % Density of PTFE [kg/m^3]
242
nu_PTFE = 0.46;
243
nu_brass = 0.35;
244
nu = nu_PTFE*ratio+nu_brass*(1-ratio);
245
radius = -0.029/2; % not the one for PTFE (it is the one for brass
)
246
elseif strcmp(chosenMaterial ,'Aluminium')
247
E = 71e9;
248
rho = 2600; % Density of aluminium [kg/m^3]
249
nu = 0.35;
250
radius = 0.025/2;
251
end
252
end

254
function fileNameStr = createNameForSimulationFile(app) % builds the
atomatic name for a simulation file based on the chosen options
255
fileNameStr = strcat('Simulation_',app.StatorMaterialDropDown.Value,'_
',app.NormalForceModelDropDown.Value,'_',app.
FrictionForceModelDropDown.Value,'_Omega_',num2str(app.
OmegaEditField.Value),'_Hz','_hitAngel_',num2str(app.
HitAngleEditField.Value),'_CoR_',num2str(app.CoREditField.Value));
256
end

258
function visualizeExperimentalData(app) % shows the magnet force data with
peaks in the GUI
259
PlotStyle
260
plot(app.UIAxes,app.Sensors.Time,app.Sensors.FT.magnet.force)
261
hold(app.UIAxes, 'on');
262
line(app.UIAxes ,[min(app.Sensors.Time), max(app.Sensors.Time)], [app.

function solvingEVP(app) % solves the eigenvalue problem
532
533
[eigvec_s ,eigval_s] = eig(app.MAT.K,app.MAT.M);
534
[eigval_s ,p] = sort(diag(eigval_s));
535
eigvec_s = eigvec_s(:,p);
536
eigfreqhz_s = sqrt(eigval_s)/(2*pi);
537
for i=1:size(eigvec_s ,2)
538
eigvec_s(:,i) = eigvec_s(:,i) ./ max(eigvec_s(:,i));
539
end

if strcmp(app.parameters.EVP,'standard')==1
542
[app.EVP.eigval,app.EVP.eigvecL ,app.EVP.eigvecR ,app.EVP.eigfreqhz ,
app.EVP.eigfreqrad] = standevp_fun(app.MAT.A,app.parameters);
543
end

[app.EVP.eigval,app.EVP.eigvecL ,app.EVP.eigvecR ,app.EVP.eigfreqhz ,
app.EVP.eigfreqrad] = generalevp_fun(app.MAT.Kglob,app.MAT.
Mglob,app.parameters);

end
